Validity of Emancipation under Duress and Intoxication
States ruling that emancipation is valid despite duress or intoxication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid emancipation when the person is under duress or intoxicated, and state the Hanafi ruling for each type.

Core Idea
Emancipation is valid under duress or prohibited intoxication, but invalid under intoxication from permissible causes.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the statement from Kanz al-Daqaiq: 'وبكره وسكر' (and under duress and intoxication). The author clarifies that duress does not invalidate emancipation, and intoxication only invalidates it when it arises from a permissible cause—not from prohibited intoxication. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of legal transactions under impaired conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Recognize that the act under discussion is emancipation ( iʿtāq ), and the default ruling is that it is valid (sahih) when all conditions are met. The question is whether duress or intoxication alter this validity.
2
Define duress and its types
Duress (ikrāh) is compelling someone to do what they do not consent to. It includes both life-threatening (mulji') and non-life-threatening duress. According to the Hanafi school, both types leave the person legally capable (ahliyyah intact), so acts performed under duress remain valid.
This differs from some other schools that may nullify actions under severe duress.
3
Classify intoxication
Intoxication (sukr) is divided into two categories: (1) prohibited intoxication from a haram substance or muthallath drunk with intent to get drunk, and (2) permissible intoxication from a forced drink, medication, or muthallath drunk for digestion, or raisin infusion without cooking. The first does not invalidate emancipation; the second invalidates it because the person is not legally responsible.
Permissible intoxication is equated with unconsciousness (ighmāʾ).
4
Apply the ruling based on intoxication type
Once you identify the cause of intoxication, apply the rule: if the intoxication is from a prohibited source or with sinful intent, emancipation is valid; if from a permissible source, emancipation is invalid. This is because in the first case the person is culpable, and in the second the person lacks legal capacity.
The same principle applies to divorce and other unilateral legal acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبكره وسكر) أي يصح العتق مع الإكراه والسكر لصدور الركن من الأهل في المحل والإكراه حمل الغير على ما لا يرضاه وأطلقه فشمل الملجيء وهو ما يفوت النفس أو العضو وغير الملجىء، وأما السكر فأطلقه أيضاً وهو مقيد بما كان من محرم أو مثلث بقصد السكر، وأما ما كان طريقه مباحاً كسكر المضطر إلى شرب الخمر والحاصل من الأدوية والأغذية المتخذة من غير العنب والمثلث لا بقصد السكر بل بقصد الاستمراء والتقوى ونقيع الزبيب بلا طبخ فإنه كالإغماء لا يصح معه تصرف ولا طلاق ولا عتاق ؛ كذا التحرير، وقدمناه في الطلاق.
His saying: (And under duress and intoxication) i.e., emancipation is valid despite duress and intoxication because the pillar is issued from one who is an eligible person in the proper place. Duress is compelling another to what he does not consent to, and he used it absolutely so it includes compelling (mulji') which takes away life or limb, and non-compelling. As for intoxication, he also used it absolutely, but it is restricted to what is from something prohibited or muthallath with the intention of intoxication. As for what is permissible in its means, like the intoxication of one forced to drink wine, and that resulting from medicines and foods taken from other than grapes, and from muthallath not with intention of intoxication but with intention of digestion and strength, and raisin infusion without cooking, then it is like unconsciousness: no transaction, divorce, or emancipation is valid with it. Thus in al-Tahrir, and we presented it in the chapter of divorce.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn statement from Kanz al-Daqaiq: 'وبكره وسكر' (and under duress and intoxication). The author explains that emancipation is valid in both cases because the essential pillar (rukn) — the utterance of emancipation — is performed by a person who is legally capable (ahli), and the place (mahall) — a valid slave — is present. Duress is defined as compelling someone to do what they do not consent to, and it includes both life-threatening (mulji') and non-life-threatening duress; in both cases emancipation is valid. Intoxication is divided into two categories: (1) Intoxication from a prohibited substance or from muthallath (a drink made from wine that has been reduced by two-thirds) when drunk with the intent to become intoxicated — here emancipation is valid. (2) Intoxication from a permissible cause, such as a forced drink, medicines, foods, or muthallath drunk for digestion, or raisin infusion without cooking — this intoxication is akin to unconsciousness (ighma'), and no legal acts (transactions, divorce, emancipation) are valid. This distinction was previously mentioned in the book of divorce. The underlying reason is that in the first case, the person is culpable for his intoxication, so his actions are effective; in the second case, he is excused, so his actions are void.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compelling someone to do something they dislike; includes both life-threatening and non-life-threatening.
السكر (al-sukr): Intoxication; here divided into prohibited (from haram or muthallath with intent) and permissible (from excusable causes).
المثلث (al-muthallath): A drink made from wine boiled down to two-thirds; its ruling depends on intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man drinks muthallath with the intention of becoming intoxicated, and then emancipates his slave. According to the passage, what is the ruling?
A) The emancipation is invalid because he was intoxicated.
B) The emancipation is valid because the intoxication was from a prohibited source with sinful intent.
C) The emancipation is valid only if the slave consents.
D) The emancipation is invalid because muthallath is haram.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, emancipation is valid under duress and prohibited intoxication, but invalid under permissible intoxication because the latter is akin to unconsciousness.

Conditional and Future Emancipation in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains validity of conditional emancipation and adding to future ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid conditional emancipation, explain the Hanafi position on adding emancipation to future ownership, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding Imam Muhammad's retraction.

Core Idea
Ta'liq is valid in emancipation because it is an isqat, but the condition must relate to ownership or be valid in isqat.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the matn's statement that adding emancipation to ownership or a condition is valid. We explore the distinction between isqat and tamlik that makes ta'liq permissible here, and examine several illustrative cases including an intra-Hanafi dispute where Imam Muhammad retracted his initial opinion. Understanding these rules prevents invalid conditional emancipations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn statement
The matn states: 'if he adds it to ownership or a condition, it is valid.' Understand the two scenarios: adding to ownership (future ownership) and adding to a condition.
2
Distinguish ta'liq in isqat vs tamlik
Emancipation is an isqat (removal), which can be conditional, unlike tamlikat (transfers). This is the key principle allowing conditional emancipation.
3
Analyze valid and invalid conditions
A condition must be a valid cause of ownership (e.g., purchase) or a condition allowed in isqat. Conditions like 'if my testator dies' are invalid because death is not a cause of ownership.
Adding to a time is like ta'liq; adding to death is tadbir.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad initially ruled that a complex conditional statement results in emancipation, but later retracted. The final mufta bihi is that neither emancipation nor divorce occurs.
Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth adopted the retracted opinion.
5
Apply to the subtle cases
Review cases from al-Zahiriyya: immediate statement 'You are free' is tanjiz; conditional gifts involving third parties; and the complex slave-girl case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن أضافه إلى ملك أو شرط صح) أي إن أضاف العتق إلى ملك بأن قال إن ملكتك فأنت حر أو إلى شرط كقوله لعبده إن دخلت الدار فأنت حر فإنه يصح ويقع العتق إذا وجد الشرط، أما الإضافة إلى الملك ففيه خلاف الشافعي وقد بيناه في كتاب الطلاق. وأما التعليق بالشرط فلأنه إسقاط فيجري فيه التعليق بخلاف التمليكات على ما عرف والإضافة إلى سبب الملك لإضافة إلى الملك كإن اشتريتك فأنت حر بخلاف إن مات مورثي فأنت ލ يصح لأن الموت لم يوضع سبباً للملك فالإضافة إلى وقت كالتعليق بالشرط من حيث إن الحكم لا يوجد فيهما إلا بعد وجود الشرط والوقت والمحل قبل ذلك على حكم ملك المالك في جميع الأحكام إلا في التعليق بشرط الموت المطلق وهو التدبير وكذا الاستيلاد؛ كذا في البدائع والتعليق بأمر كائن تنجيز قال في الظهيرية : لو قال لعبده إن حر عتق للحال بخلاف قوله لمكاتبه إن أنت فأنت عبدي ލ لا يعتق . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : وبه نأخذ لأن في الإضافة قصوراً .اهـ. وفيها أيضاً: رجل قال لعبد رجل إن وهبك مولاك لي فأنت حر فوهبه له والعبد في يد الواهب لا يعتق قبل أو لم يقبل، وكذا لو كان العبد في يد الموهوب له وقد ابتدأ الواهب بالهبة قبل الموهوب له أو لم يقبل، وإن ابتدأ الموهوب له فقال هب لي هذا العبد والعبد في يد الموهوب له فقال صاحب العبد وهبت لك عتق اهـ. ومن مسائل التعليق اللطيفة ما في الظهيرية رجل قال لأمته إذا مات والدي فأنت حرة ثم باعها من والده ثم تزوجها ثم قال لها إن مات والدي فأنت طالق ثنتين فمات الوالد كان محمد رحمه الله تعالى يقول أولاً تعتق ولا تطلق ثم رجع وقال : لا يقع طلاق ولا عتاق والمسألة على الاستقصاء في المبسوط اهـ.
His saying: (And if he adds it to ownership or a condition, it is valid) i.e., if he adds emancipation to ownership by saying 'If I own you, you are free,' or to a condition like saying to his slave 'If you enter the house, you are free,' then it is valid and emancipation occurs when the condition is found. Regarding adding to ownership, there is a difference with al-Shafi'i, which we explained in the book of divorce. As for conditional suspension (ta'liq), because it is a removal (isqat), suspension applies to it unlike transfers of ownership as known. Adding to a cause of ownership is like adding to ownership, such as 'If I buy you, you are free,' unlike 'If my testator dies, you are free' which is not valid because death is not made a cause for ownership. Adding to a time is like conditional suspension in that the ruling does not exist except after the existence of the condition and time, and the subject before that is under the rule of the owner's ownership in all rulings, except in suspension by the absolute condition of death, which is tadbir, and likewise istilad. So in al-Bada'i. Suspension by an existing matter is immediate (tanjiz). It is said in al-Zahiriyya: If he says to his slave 'You are already free,' it is immediate emancipation, unlike his statement to his mukatab 'If you are, you are my slave' does not make him free. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: We adopt this because in addition there is deficiency. Also in it: A man said to a man's slave, 'If your master gives you to me, you are free,' then he gave him to him, and the slave is in the hand of the donor, it does not become free, whether he accepted or not. Likewise if the slave is in the hand of the recipient, and the donor began the gift before the recipient accepted or not. But if the recipient began by saying 'Give me this slave' and the slave is in the hand of the recipient, and the owner of the slave said 'I gave you,' then he is free. Among the subtle conditional issues is what is in al-Zahiriyya: A man said to his slave-girl, 'When my father dies, you are free.' Then he sold her to his father, then married her, then said to her, 'If my father dies, you are divorced by two.' Then the father died. Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) used to say first: She is free but not divorced. Then he retracted and said: Neither divorce nor emancipation occurs. The matter is elaborated in al-Mabsut.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage explains the matn statement 'وإن أضافه إلى ملك أو شرط صح' (if he adds emancipation to ownership or a condition, it is valid). The author discusses two scenarios: (1) Adding to ownership: e.g., saying 'If I own you, you are free' is valid, but al-Shafi'i disagreed (this is explained in the book of divorce). (2) Adding to a condition: e.g., 'If you enter the house, you are free' is valid because emancipation is an isqat (removal of ownership), and isqat can be suspended, unlike tamlikat (transfers of ownership) which cannot be suspended. Adding to a cause of ownership (e.g., 'If I buy you') is like adding to ownership. Adding to a time (e.g., 'If my father dies') is valid only if it is equal to tadbir (i.e., suspension by death). Adding to a pure condition that is not a cause of ownership, like 'If my testator dies,' is invalid because death is not a cause of ownership. The text then provides several subtle cases from al-Zahiriyya: (a) A man says to his slave 'You are free' (tanjiz) is immediate, unlike saying to a mukatab 'You are my slave' which does not make him free. (b) A conditional gift involving a third party's slave. (c) A complex case where a man says to his slave-girl 'When my father dies, you are free,' then sells her to his father, marries her, then says 'If my father dies, you are divorced by two.' Imam Muhammad initially ruled that she becomes free but not divorced, then retracted to say neither occurs. This shows the subtlety of conditional emancipation and the Hanafi position that it is valid only when properly structured.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'līq): Suspending the effect of a legal act on the occurrence of a future condition.
الإسقاط (al-isqāṭ): Removal of a right or ownership; emancipation is an isqat.
التدبير (al-tadbīr): Suspending emancipation upon the death of the owner.
التنجيز (al-tanjīz): Immediate execution of a legal act without condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his slave: 'If my testator dies, you are free.' According to the text, this statement is:
A) Valid, and the slave becomes free upon the testator's death.
B) Invalid, because death is not a cause of ownership.
C) Valid only if the slave is the heir.
D) Invalid, because it is a future condition.

Key Takeaway
Ta'liq is valid in emancipation because it is isqat, but only when the condition is a valid cause of ownership or allowed in isqat; the Hanafi school allows adding to future ownership, while Shafi'i disagrees; intra-Hanafi disputes must be resolved by following the final mufta bihi.

Emancipation of a Pregnant Slave and Implicit Ownership in Freeing Another's Slave
Expansion on emancipation rulings with intra-Hanafi dispute and a case of implicit ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on freeing a pregnant slave, explain Abu Yusuf's exception regarding the emergence of most of the child, and distinguish between implicit and explicit emancipations when freeing another's slave.

Core Idea
Fetus follows mother in emancipation, but if most of the child has emerged, it is separate; freeing another's slave establishes ownership implicitly, unlike explicit declarations.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents two distinct but related issues: the extent to which a fetus is included in the emancipation of its mother, and the legal effect of freeing a slave belonging to another person. The first involves an intra-Hanafi nuance from Abu Yusuf, while the second introduces the important fiqh principle that implicit matters (ḍimniyyāt) are treated with leniency compared to explicit intentions (qaṣdiyyāt). Understanding these rulings sharpens one's grasp of how Hanafi jurists handle tacit consequences and exceptions in emancipation.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling on pregnant slave
From the matn, identify the ruling when a pregnant slave is freed. What happens to the mother and the fetus?
The matn says 'ولو حرر حاملاً عتقاً' meaning both are free.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's exception
Abu Yusuf adds a detail: if most of the child has emerged (i.e., during birth), then freeing the mother does not free the child. Why is the child considered 'like separated'?
The child in that state is legally separate for rulings like iddah and inheritance.
3
Analyze the evidence for the exception
Examine the analogy: if the mother dies after most of the child has emerged, the child inherits; if before, it does not. Also, the iddah ends with the birth. How do these support Abu Yusuf's view?
These show the child is treated as a separate legal entity when most has emerged.
4
Examine the second case from al-Khaniyyah
What happens if a man frees a slave-girl owned by another? How is ownership established?
The ownership is established implicitly (ḍimnan) within the emancipation itself, to remove disgrace from a relative.
5
Distinguish implicit vs explicit
Why is the implicit emancipation valid while saying 'Every slave I own is free' is not? Compare the two types of statements.
Implicit matters allow leniency not permitted in explicit intentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو حرر حاملاً عتقاً) أي الأم والحمل تبعاً لها إذ هو متصل بها فهو كسائر أجزائها ولو استثناه لا يصح كاستثناء جزء منها. وقال أبو يوسف : إذا خرج أكثر الولد فأعتق الأم لا يعتق الولد لأنه كالمنفصل في حق الأحكام ألا ترى أنه تنقضي به العدة ولو مات في هذه الحالة يرث بخلاف ما إذا مات قبل خروج الأكثر ؛ هكذا ذكره الشارحون وظاهره أن نسبة هذا التفصيل لأبي يوسف لكونه نقل عنه وحده لا لأن الصاحبين يخالفانه فإنه موافق للقاعدة. وفي الخانية: رجل أعتق جارية إنسان الملك الثابت هنا إنما هو في ضمن ثبوت العتق المحكوم بثبوته شرعاً لضرورة دفع الذل عن القريب قرابة قوية ويغتفر في الضمنيات ما لا يغتفر في القصديات بخلاف قوله كل مملوك لي حر فإنه قصدي
His saying: (And if he frees a pregnant woman, both are free) i.e., the mother and the fetus follow her, for it is connected to her, so it is like the rest of her parts. If he excludes it, it is not valid, like excluding a part of her. Abu Yusuf said: If most of the child has emerged, then he frees the mother, the child is not freed because it is like separated in terms of rulings. Do you not see that the waiting period ends with it, and if he dies in this state, he inherits, unlike if he dies before the majority emerges. Thus the commentators mentioned it, and it appears that this detail is attributed to Abu Yusuf alone, not because the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, for it is consistent with the principle. In al-Khaniyyah: A man freed a slave-girl of someone else; the ownership established here is within the establishment of the emancipation ruled to be valid legally due to the necessity of removing disgrace from a relative with strong kinship, and allowances are made in implicit matters that are not made in explicit intentions, unlike saying 'Every slave I own is free,' which is explicit.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rulings. First, the matn statement 'ولو حرر حاملاً عتقاً' meaning if one frees a pregnant female slave, both mother and unborn child are free; the child follows the mother as part of her. Excluding the child is invalid, like excluding a limb. Then Abu Yusuf adds a detail: if most of the child has already emerged (i.e., during birth), then freeing the mother does not free the child, because it is legally considered separate (e.g., iddah ends, inheritance applies). This is reported only from Abu Yusuf, but it is based on the general principle and not contradicted by Muhammad. Second, a separate case from al-Khaniyyah: If a man frees a slave-girl owned by another person, the ownership (for the freed) is established implicitly within the establishment of the emancipation, legally valid for the necessity of removing disgrace from a close relative. This is allowed because implicit matters are given leniency that explicit intentions are not. Contrast with saying 'Every slave I own is free,' which is an explicit intention and requires actual ownership. The text thus distinguishes between implicit and explicit emancipations.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-ḥaml): The fetus or unborn child of a pregnant slave.
الضمنيات (al-ḍimniyyāt): Implicit matters; things that are established as a consequence of another ruling.
القصديات (al-qasadiyyāt): Explicit intentions; directly stated matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Khaniyyah, if a man frees a slave-girl owned by another, the emancipation is valid because:
A) The man becomes the owner of the slave-girl by the act of freeing.
B) The slave-girl is considered a relative of the man.
C) Ownership is established implicitly within the emancipation to remove disgrace from a relative.
D) The man's intention overrides lack of ownership.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a fetus is freed with its mother as part of her, but if most of the child has emerged, it is treated separately; and freeing another's slave can be valid through implicit ownership when necessary to remove disgrace from a close relative.

Emancipation of the Fetus: The Six-Month Rule and Its Exceptions
Commentary on emancipation of the fetus with six-month distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emancipating a fetus, apply the six-month rule to determine independent vs. subordinate emancipation, and identify the destination of clientage (wala').

Core Idea
A fetus is freed independently only if born within six months; otherwise it is a subordinate, shifting clientage to the father's master.

When a master declares 'what is in your womb is free,' the timing of birth determines not only the child's freedom but also the legal implications of clientage. The Hanafi school uses a six-month threshold to ascertain whether the fetus existed at the time of the declaration, a principle rooted in medical knowledge of the minimum gestation period. This lesson unpacks the commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq, clarifying the main rule, its exceptions, and two practical derivative cases.

Guided Path
1
State the core ruling
Identify the central ruling: if a master says 'what is in your womb is free,' the fetus is emancipated, but the mode (independent vs. subordinate) depends on birth timing.
2
Apply the six-month condition
Understand that birth within six months of the declaration proves existence at the time, leading to independent emancipation. Birth at six months or more indicates the fetus may not have existed, so emancipation is by subordination (only if the mother is also free).
The six-month period is based on the minimum duration of pregnancy.
3
Determine clientage (wala')
For independent emancipation, wala' goes to the master (emancipator). For subordinate emancipation, wala' goes to the father's master (if the father is a slave) or to the father's free status.
Wala' is the legal relationship of inheritance between emancipator and freed slave.
4
Identify the exceptions
Note two exceptions where a fetus born after six months is still considered to have existed: (1) the mother is in iddah (up to two years after separation), (2) she gives birth to twins and the first is born within six months.
These exceptions rely on evidence of existence from lineage or the first twin.
5
Apply the rule to derivative issues
Work through the two derivative cases: (a) mixed declarations ('what's in your womb is free' then 'if you become pregnant, Salim is free'), and (b) injury causing miscarriage. Use the six-month rule to determine outcomes.
In case (a), the master's explanation is accepted. In case (b), blood money is due only if the fetus was emancipated (i.e., born within six months).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاملاً عتقا وإن حرره عتق فقط والولد يتبع أمه في الملك والحرية والرق والتدبير والأستيلاد والكتابة وولد الأمة من سيدها حر. فأجاز المولى إعتاقه بعد ما ولدت يعتق الولد .اهـ. وأطلق المصنف في عتق الحمل فشمل ما إذا ولدته بعد عتقها لستة أشهر أو أقل أو أكثر لكن إن ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر بعد عتقها فإنه يعتق مقصوداً لا بطريق التبعية حتى لا ينجر ولاؤه إلى مولى الأب وإن ولدته لستة أشهر فأكثر فإنه يعتق بطريق التبعية فحينئذ ينجر الولاء إلى مولى الأب كما في شرح الوقاية . وعلى هذا فينبغي أن يحمل قوله هنا على ما إذا ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر ليكون عتقه بطريق الأصالة لئلا يلزم التكرار ولأنه سيذكر أن الولد يتبع الأم في الحرية والتبعية إنما تكون إذا ولدته لستة أشهر فأكثر فيحمل عليه اللهم إلا أن يريد بالحرية الحرية الأصلية فلا إشكال ولا تکرار . قوله : (وإن حرره عتق فقط) أي إن حرر الحمل وحده عتق هو دون أمه لأنه لا وجه إلى إعتاقها مقصود العدم الإضافة إليها ولا إليه تبعاً لما فيه من قلب الموضوع. ثم إعتاق الحمل صحيح ولا يصح بيعه ولا هبته لأن التسليم نفسه شرط في الهبة والقدرة عليه في البيع ولم يوجد بالإضافة إلى الجنين وشيء من ذلك ليس شرطاً في الإعتاق فافترقا . وأفاد بقوله «حرره» أنه كان موجوداً وقت التحرير ولن يتحقق وجوده إلا إذا ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر، وإن ولدته لستة أشهر فأكثر فإنه لا يعتق ولا يكون قوله ما في بطنك حر إقراراً بوجوده لعدم التيقن بوجوده وقته لجواز حدوثه إلا في مسألتين: إحداهما ما إذا كانت الأمة معتدة عن طلاق أو وفاة فتلده لأقل من سنتين من وقت الفراق، وإن كان لأكثر من ستة أشهر من وقت الإعتاق فحينئذ يعتق لأنه كان موجوداً حين أعتقه بدليل ثبوت نسبه . ثانيهما إذا كان حملها توأمين فجاءت بأولهما لأقل من ستة أشهر ثم جاءت بالثاني لستة أشهر أو أكثر فإنه يعتق لأنه كان محكوماً بوجوده حين أعتقه حتى ثبت نسبه. وتفرع على التفصيل السابق مسألتان : إحداهما لو قال المولى ما في بطنك حر ثم قال إن حملت فسالم حر فولدت بعده لستة أشهر فالقول له إن أقر أنها كانت حاملاً يومئذ عتق الولد وإن أقر أنه حمل مستقبل عتق لأنا تيقنا بعتق أحدهما وشككنا في الآخر لأنه لا يخلوا إما أن يكون العلوق والحمل كان موجوداً وقت الإعتاق أو كان حادثاً بعده فرجع في البيان إليه. وإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين يعتق سالم دون الولد لأنا تيقنا أنه لم يكن موجوداً وقت الإعتاق، وإن جاءت لأقل مطلق فيقتضي صفة الكمال فاحتاج إلى الملك المطلق ولم يكن فيه مطلق الملك، ألا ترى أنه لا يدخل فيه البعض المملوك ويدخل في ملك القريب فيعتق والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم. قوله: (وينبغي أن يقال إن ولدته الخ لأنه قد يكون الضرب بعد العتق لأقل من ستة أشهر...ستة أشهر يعتق الولد دون سالم لأنا تيقنا أنه كان موجوداً وقت الإعتاق. ثانيهما لو قال من في بطنك حر ثم ضرب بطنها فألقت جنيناً ميتاً إن ضربها بعد العتق لأقل من ستة أشهر تجب دية الجنين الحر لأبيه إن كان له أب حر، وإن لم يكن يكون لعصبة المولى لأن المولى قاتل فلا يستحق الميراث، وإن ضرب لستة أشهر لا شيء عليه لأنه لم يعتق؛ كذا في المحيط . وينبغي أن يقال : إن ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر بعد العتق أو لستة أشهر ولا يذكر الضرب إذ لا دخل له .
If she is pregnant and he emancipates it (the fetus) only, the child is emancipated and follows its mother in ownership, freedom, slavery, tadbir, istilad, and kitaba; and the child of an umm walad from her master is free. So the master permits its emancipation after it is born; the child is emancipated. The author (al-Mawsili) spoke generally in the emancipation of the fetus, including if she gives birth after her emancipation at six months or less or more. But if she gives birth at less than six months after her emancipation, then it is emancipated as an independent act, not by way of subordination, so that its wala' does not transfer to the master of the father. If she gives birth at six months or more, then it is emancipated by way of subordination; then the wala' transfers to the master of the father, as in Sharh al-Wiqaya. Based on this, his statement here should be understood as applying when she gives birth at less than six months, so that its emancipation is by way of originality to avoid repetition, and because he will mention that the child follows the mother in freedom, and subordination only occurs when she gives birth at six months or more, so it is carried upon that. However, if he intends by freedom the original freedom, then there is no problem and no repetition. His statement: (And if he emancipates it alone, it is freed only) means if he emancipates the fetus alone, it becomes free without its mother, because there is no way to intend her emancipation without specification to her, nor to intend it as a subordinate due to the reversal of the subject. Then the emancipation of the fetus is valid, and its sale or gift is not valid because delivery itself is a condition in gift, and the ability to deliver is a condition in sale, and neither exists with respect to the fetus; but none of that is a condition in emancipation, so they differ. His word 'emancipates it' indicates that it existed at the time of emancipation, and its existence is only verified if she gives birth at less than six months. If she gives birth at six months or more, then it is not emancipated, and his statement 'what is in your womb is free' is not an acknowledgment of its existence because there is no certainty of its existence at that time, as it might have occurred later, except in two cases: first, if the slave woman is in iddah from divorce or death and she gives birth at less than two years from the time of separation, even if it is more than six months from the time of emancipation, then it is emancipated because it existed at the time he emancipated it, as proven by establishing lineage. Second, if her pregnancy is twins and she delivers the first at less than six months and then the second at six months or more, then it is emancipated because it was deemed to exist at the time of emancipation until its lineage was established. Based on the previous detail, two issues derive: one, if the master said 'what is in your womb is free', then said 'if you become pregnant, Salim is free', and she gives birth after six months; then the statement is for him: if he acknowledges that she was pregnant at that time, the child is free; if he acknowledges that it is a future pregnancy, Salim is free, because we are certain of the emancipation of one and doubt the other, as it cannot be but that the conception and pregnancy existed at the time of emancipation or occurred afterwards, so he has the say in clarification. If she brings it after more than two years, Salim is emancipated without the child, because we are certain it did not exist at the time of emancipation. If she brings it within less than that absolutely, it requires perfection of attribute and thus requires absolute ownership, and absolute ownership was not present; do you not see that the partially owned part does not enter into it but enters into the ownership of a relative, so it is emancipated. And Allah knows best. His statement: (And it should be said that if she gives birth to it... because the beating may be after emancipation at less than six months... six months, the child is emancipated without Salim, because we are certain it existed at the time of emancipation. Second, if he said 'who is in your womb is free' then he struck her belly and she miscarried a dead fetus: if he struck after emancipation at less than six months, the blood money for a free fetus is obligatory for his father if he has a free father; otherwise it goes to the agnates of the master because the master is a killer and does not inherit. If he struck at six months, nothing is upon him because it was not emancipated. So in al-Muhit. And it should be said: if she gives birth at less than six months after emancipation or at six months, and the beating is not mentioned, it has no bearing.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, expanding the ruling on emancipating the fetus (al-haml). The author, Zayn al-Din al-Razi, explains that when a master says 'what is in your womb is free,' the fetus is freed, but the timing of birth determines whether it is an original emancipation or subsidiary (taba'iyyah). If born within six months, it existed at the time of declaration, so it is freed independently and its wala' goes to the master (not the father's master). If born after six months, it is considered to have been conceived after the declaration, so it is freed only as a subsidiary (i.e., because its mother is freed) and its wala' goes to the father's master. The author clarifies exceptions: (1) the mother in iddah (she could be pregnant from the former husband), so even if born after six months, if within two years, the fetus existed; (2) twins — if the first is born within six months, the second is also considered existing. Two derivative issues are discussed: (a) mixed declarations ('what's in your womb is free' then 'if you get pregnant, Salim is free') — if born after six months, the master's explanation is accepted (whether it was the first or second pregnancy); (b) injury causing miscarriage — blood money (diya) for a free fetus is due if the beating was within six months of emancipation (indicating existence), otherwise nothing. The passage relies on istihsan (juristic preference) based on the principle that existence is determined by the possibility of conception at the time of the oath.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): The fetus in the womb; legally, a potential human being with certain rights.
تبعية (taba'iyyah): Subordination; the fetus is freed as a subordinate to the mother's emancipation.
ولاء (wala'): Clientage; the legal relationship between the emancipator and the freed slave, affecting inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his pregnant slave woman 'what is in your womb is free,' and she gives birth exactly six months after that declaration, what is the ruling on the child?
A) The child is free independently, and its wala' goes to the master.
B) The child is free independently, and its wala' goes to the father's master.
C) The child is free by subordination, and its wala' goes to the master.
D) The child is free by subordination, and its wala' goes to the father's master.

Key Takeaway
The six-month rule determines whether a fetus declared free is freed independently or as a subordinate, affecting clientage.

Further Scenarios of Fetus Emancipation: Conditions, Payment, and Joint Ownership
Rulings on conditional, payment-based, and joint-ownership fetus emancipation with qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on conditional and payment-based emancipation of a fetus, and calculate liability in joint-ownership scenarios.

Core Idea
A fetus cannot be charged, but its emancipation can be suspended on conditions; partial owners face liability only if born alive.

The previous lesson covered basic emancipation of a pregnant slave’s fetus. This lesson expands into trickier scenarios: what if the master attaches a condition or demands payment? What if the fetus is owned jointly? The Hanafi school uses qiyas (analogy) to resolve these cases—treating the fetus as a free person regarding payment, and the partial emancipator as a mukatib for injury diya. We will walk through these rulings as recorded in al-Bada’i‘, al-Hidaya, and al-Muhit.

Guided Path
1
Classify the emancipation scenario
Identify whether the master made the emancipation conditional on birth (‘if you give birth’), linked to payment, or involving joint ownership. Each has distinct rules.
2
Determine timing and living condition
Note that the fetus must be born within six months of the oath (to confirm existence) and, for unconditional emancipation, must be born alive for the master to be liable. For conditional cases, the condition governs timing.
3
Analyze liability for payment
When emancipation is for a price, the payment is never due from the fetus (no capacity) nor from the mother (separate entity). If she voluntarily accepts, the child is freed and the payment is void because the condition is invalid.
4
Apply the rule for joint ownership and outside harm
If one partner emancipates the fetus and it is born dead, he is not liable (doubt about life). If born alive, he is liable for his share. If an outsider strikes the mother and she miscarries, the outsider pays diya proportionally according to Abu Hanifa, treating the partial emancipator like a mukatib.
5
Synthesize using qiyas
Recognize the key analogies: (1) The fetus’s lack of legal capacity prevents imposing payment—like a free person. (2) The partial emancipator’s liability for injury is qiyas to a mukatib (slave under contract), whose diya is calculated based on his free portion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البدائع : وكذا إذا قال إذا ولدت ما في بطنك فهو حر لا يعتق حتى تلده لأقل ستة أشهر من يوم . حلف للتيقن بوجوده قبل الحلف إلا أن ههنا يعتق من حين حلف، وفي إذا ولدت ما في بطنك من يوم تلد لاشتراطه الولادة .اهـ وأطلق المصنف في عتق الحمل فشمل ما إذا أعتقه على مال فإنه يصح ولا يجب المال إذ لا وجه إلى إلزام المال على الجنين لعدم الولاية عليه ولا إلى إلزامه الأم لأنه في حق العتق نفس على حدة واشتراط بدل العتق على غير المعتق لا يجوز على ما مر في الخلع ؛ كذا في الهداية لكن لو أعتقه على مال على أمه لا بد من قبولها لعتقه وإن لم يلزمها شيء لما في المحيط : ولو قال أعتقت ما في بطنك على ألف عليك فقبلت فجاءت بولد لأقل من ستة أشهر يعتق بلا شيء لأن العتق معلق بقبول الأمة الألف وقد قبلت الألف فعتق الولد وبطل المال .اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : لو قال لأمته ما في بطنك حر متى أدى إلي ألفاً أو إذا أدى إلي ألفاً فوضعت لأقل من ستة أشهر فهو حر متى أدى إليه ألف درهم وأطلق في تحرير الحمل فشمل ما إذا قال حملك حر أو ما في بطنك حر أو قال العلقة أو المضغة التي في بطنك حر فإنه يعتق ما في بطنها؛ كذا في الخانية. ولو قال أكبر ولد في بطنك فهو حر فولدت ولدين في بطن فأولهما خروجاً أكبرهما وليس منه إن ولدت ولداً كذا في المحيط. وكذا لو قال إن حملت بولد فهو حر وهو حر : لأنه لا يعتق إلا بعد الولادة حتى لو باع الأم أو مات المولى قبل الولادة بطلت اليمين كما في البدائع . ولم يشترط المصنف ولادته حياً بعد عتقه وظاهر ما في المحيط أنه شرط قال : ولو أعتق أحد شريكي الأمة ما في بطنها فولدت توأماً ميتاً لا ضمان عليه لأن الإتلاف لم يثبت يقيناً لاحتمال أن الجنين لم يكن حياً ولم تنفخ فيه الروح أصلاً فلا يجب الضمان بالشك. ولو ولدت توأماً حياً يضمن لأن الظاهر أن الحياة كانت موجودة فيه وقت الإعتاق، ولو أعتق أحد الشريكين الجنين فضرب أجنبي بطنها وألقت ميتاً فعلى الضارب نصف عشر قيمته إن كان غلاماً، وعشر قيمتها إن كانت جارية عند أبي حنيفة لأن معتق البعض كالمكاتب
In al-Bada'i': Similarly, if he says 'if you give birth to what is in your womb, he is free,' it is not emancipated until she gives birth at less than six months from the day he swore, for certainty of its existence before the oath; except that here it is emancipated from the time he swore, whereas in 'if you give birth to what is in your womb' it is from the day she gives birth due to stipulating birth. The author (al-Mawsili) spoke generally in the emancipation of the fetus, so it includes if he emancipates it for payment: it is valid and the payment is not obligatory, because there is no way to impose payment on the fetus due to lack of guardianship over it, nor to impose it on the mother because in the matter of emancipation it is a separate entity, and stipulating a price for emancipation on someone other than the freed person is not permissible, as passed in khulu'. Thus in al-Hidaya. But if he emancipates it for payment on his mother, her acceptance of his emancipation is necessary, even if it does not bind her anything. As in al-Muhit: If he said 'I emancipate what is in your womb for one thousand upon you' and she accepted, and she gives birth to a child at less than six months, it is emancipated without anything, because the emancipation was suspended upon the slave woman's acceptance of the thousand, and she accepted the thousand, so the child is freed and the payment becomes void. In al-Zahiriyya: If he said to his slave woman 'what is in your womb is free whenever he pays me a thousand' or 'if he pays me a thousand', and she gives birth at less than six months, then he is free whenever he pays the thousand dirhams. And the author's generalization in emancipation of the fetus includes if he said 'your pregnancy is free' or 'what is in your womb is free' or said 'the leech or the clump that is in your womb is free' — then what is in her womb is freed. Thus in al-Khaniyya. If he said 'the eldest child in your womb is free' and she gives birth to two children in one womb, the one who comes out first is the elder and not from him if she gives birth to one child. Thus in al-Muhit. Similarly, if he said 'if you become pregnant with a child, he is free' — it is not emancipated except after birth, so if he sells the mother or the master dies before birth, the oath becomes void, as in al-Bada'i'. The author did not stipulate that the child be born alive after its emancipation, but apparently in al-Muhit it is a condition. He said: If one of the two joint owners of a slave woman emancipates what is in her womb, and she gives birth to dead twins, there is no liability upon him because loss (itlaf) is not established with certainty, since it is possible that the fetus was not alive and the soul had not been breathed into it, so liability is not obligatory due to doubt. If she gives birth to live twins, he is liable because the apparent state is that life existed at the time of emancipation. If one of the two partners emancipates the fetus, and an outsider strikes her belly and she miscarries a dead fetus, then upon the striker is half a tenth of his value if it was a male, and a tenth of her value if it was a female, according to Abu Hanifa, because the partial emancipator is like a mukatib.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the commentary on fetus emancipation, addressing additional scenarios. First, if the master makes emancipation conditional on birth (e.g., 'if you give birth, it is free'), the child is only freed after birth, and only if born within six months of the oath (ensuring existence). However, the timing of the emancipation differs: if the condition is 'if you give birth...', it becomes free at birth; if the condition is 'when you give birth...', it is considered suspended and becomes free from the time of the oath? Actually, the text explains that in 'if you give birth' it is from the day of birth, while in 'when you give birth' it is from the time of the oath? The translation is tricky: 'في إذا ولدت ما في بطنك من يوم تلد' means in the case of 'if you give birth' it is from the day she gives birth. Next, the author discusses emancipation for a price: if the master says 'I emancipate what is in your womb for one thousand', it is valid but no payment is due from the fetus (no capacity) nor from the mother (since she is not being freed). However, if the mother accepts to pay the thousand, the fetus is freed and the payment is void (because the condition is invalid; the emancipation is valid without payment). Then, the text covers specifications: if the master says 'your pregnancy is free' or 'the leech (alaqah) or clump (mudghah) is free', it all counts as emancipation of the fetus. If he says 'the eldest child in your womb is free' and she gives birth to twins, the first-born is the eldest. If he says 'if you become pregnant, the child is free', it is only effective upon birth, and if the mother is sold or the master dies before birth, the oath is void. Finally, the text deals with joint ownership: if one partner emancipates the fetus, and it is born dead, there is no liability because it might not have been alive; if born alive, the partner is liable. If the fetus is emancipated by one partner and then an outsider causes a miscarriage, the outsider pays a diya (blood money) based on value, because the partial emancipator is like a mukatib (who is not fully free).

Key Terms
علقة (alaqah): A leech-like stage of embryonic development; here used as a synonym for fetus in early pregnancy.
مضغة (mudghah): A clump of flesh; a stage of embryonic development after the alaqah.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn and pay for his freedom; here, the partial emancipator is analogized to a mukatib in terms of liability for injury to the fetus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a joint owner of a slave woman says 'I emancipate the fetus in her womb,' and the fetus is born dead, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The joint owner must pay the full value of the fetus.
B) The joint owner must pay half the value of the fetus.
C) The joint owner is not liable because the fetus might not have been alive.
D) The joint owner is liable only if the mother was also emancipated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses qiyas (to a free person and a mukatib) to rule that a fetus cannot be charged for its freedom, and joint owners face liability only if the fetus is born alive.

Emancipation of the Fetus and the Six-Month Rule
Explains the six-month rule for emancipation of fetus and effects of striking after emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi six-month rule for emancipation of a fetus and apply it to determine the validity of emancipation and liability for miscarriage.

Core Idea
Emancipation of a fetus is valid only if born within six months — the key to both freedom and blood-money liability.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a crucial Hanafi rule: when a master emancipates the fetus of his pregnant slave-girl, the child becomes free only if born within six months. This six-month window also determines whether the master is liable for blood-money if he causes a miscarriage. The reasoning is certainty of the fetus's existence at the time of the statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the emancipation statement type
Determine whether the master said 'ما في بطنك حر' (direct emancipation) or 'إذا ولدت ما في بطنك فهو حر' (conditional upon birth). The timing of freedom differs.
Direct: freedom takes effect immediately but contingent on birth within six months. Conditional: freedom takes effect at birth, but still requires birth within six months.
2
Apply the six-month period
Count six months from the moment of the statement (for direct) or from the oath (for conditional). If birth occurs before six months exactly, the child is free. If at six months or later, the child is not free.
The text explicitly says 'at six months' the child is not emancipated.
3
Determine the child's status
If born within six months, the child is free (emancipated from the time of the statement or from birth, depending on the wording). If at six months or later, the child remains a slave.
4
Analyze the striking scenario
If the master strikes the slave after the emancipation statement and she miscarries within six months, the master must pay the diyah of a free child (ghurrah). If at six months, no diyah because the child was never free.
The master cannot inherit, so the diyah goes to the child's agnatic relatives ('asaba).
5
Distinguish direct and conditional wording
With direct 'ما في بطنك حر', emancipation is immediate (though contingent). With conditional 'إذا ولدت...', emancipation occurs at birth, but the six-month limit still applies from the oath.
The key difference: timing of when freedom is considered effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ستة أشهر يعتق الولد دون سالم لأنا تيقنا أنه كان موجوداً وقت الإعتاق. ثانيهما لو قال من في بطنك حر ثم ضرب بطنها فألقت جنيناً ميتاً إن ضربها بعد العتق لأقل من ستة أشهر تجب دية الجنين الحر لأبيه إن كان له أب حر، وإن لم يكن يكون لعصبة المولى لأن المولى قاتل فلا يستحق الميراث، وإن ضرب لستة أشهر لا شيء عليه لأنه لم يعتق؛ كذا في المحيط . وينبغي أن يقال : إن ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر بعد العتق أو لستة أشهر ولا يذكر الضرب إذ لا دخل له . من وفي البدائع : وكذا إذا قال إذا ولدت ما في بطنك فهو حر لا يعتق حتى تلده لأقل ستة أشهر من يوم . حلف للتيقن بوجوده قبل الحلف إلا أن ههنا يعتق من حين حلف، وفي إذا ولدت ما في بطنك من يوم تلد لاشتراطه الولادة .اهـ
At six months, the child is emancipated without [the condition of] safety because we are certain that it existed at the time of emancipation. Second: if he said 'whoever is in your womb is free' then struck her belly and she miscarried a dead fetus, if he struck her after the emancipation at less than six months, the blood-money of the free fetus is obligatory for his father if he has a free father, and if not, it goes to the 'asaba of the master because the master is a killer so he does not inherit. And if he struck at six months, nothing is due because he was not emancipated; thus in al-Muhit. And it should be said: if she gives birth to it at less than six months after emancipation or at six months, and the striking is not mentioned, it has no bearing. And in al-Bada'i: similarly, if he said 'when you give birth to what is in your womb, it is free', it is not emancipated until she gives birth to it at less than six months from the day he swore, to be certain of its existence before the oath. Except that here it is emancipated from the time of his oath, while in 'if you give birth to what is in your womb' it is from the day she gives birth because he made birth a condition.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage deals with a master who says to his pregnant slave-girl 'ما في بطنك حر' (what is in your womb is free). The key ruling is that if the baby is born within six months of that statement, it is free because we are certain the fetus existed at the time of the statement. If born after six months, the child is not free. The author then discusses the case where the master strikes the slave-girl after the emancipation statement and she miscarries. If the miscarriage occurs within six months (so the fetus was definitely free at the time of striking), the master is liable for the blood-money (diyah) of a free child, which goes to the child's agnatic relatives ('asaba) because the master as the killer does not inherit. If the miscarriage occurs at six months or later (so the child was never free), no liability. Al-Bada'i adds that if the master says 'إذا ولدت ما في بطنك فهو حر' (when you give birth to what is in your womb, it is free), emancipation occurs at birth, but only if birth happens within six months of the oath; otherwise it is void. The key difference is that in the direct statement, freedom takes effect immediately; in the conditional statement, it takes effect at birth.

Key Terms
دية الجنين (diyat al-janin): Blood-money payable for causing miscarriage of a free fetus: a ghurrah (500 dirhams or 5 camels) if dead, or full diyah if born alive and then dies
عتق الحمل (itq al-haml): Emancipation of the fetus in a pregnant slave-girl's womb; it takes effect if born within six months

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his pregnant slave-girl 'ما في بطنك حر' and she gives birth after seven months, what is the ruling?
A) The child is free because the master intended emancipation.
B) The child is not free because it was born after six months, so the fetus was not certainly present at the time of statement.
C) The child is free, but the master must pay compensation.
D) The child remains a slave because emancipation of fetus is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The six-month rule determines both the validity of fetal emancipation and the liability for blood-money if the child is miscarried within that period.

Emancipation of the Fetus in Exchange for Payment
Rules on emancipating the fetus in return for payment, and on conditional payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on emancipating a fetus in exchange for payment and explain why the payment is not enforceable on the fetus or mother.

Core Idea
Emancipating a fetus for payment is valid, but the payment is void because the fetus cannot be held liable.

This lesson explores a unique ruling: how the Hanafi school handles the emancipation of an unborn child when a condition of payment is attached. The key principle is that the fetus is a separate entity for purposes of emancipation, and cannot be burdened with a debt. We will examine the distinction between conditions imposed on the fetus, on the mother, and on a third party, and how each affects the validity of the emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The passage begins by stating that the author's general wording about emancipation of the fetus includes cases where it is done in exchange for property. The act is valid (sahih), but the payment is not obligatory. Understand why: no guardianship over the fetus to impose a debt, and the fetus is a separate soul from the mother.
This is a foundational rule: a fetus cannot be held financially liable.
2
Analyze the condition of payment on the mother
If the master says 'I emancipate your child on condition that you (the mother) pay X', her acceptance is necessary, but even then, no payment is due from her. The condition is void because it is like imposing a price for emancipation on someone who is not being emancipated.
Refer to the analogy with khul' mentioned in the text.
3
Differentiate the conditional emancipation on the child itself
When the master says 'what is in your womb is free when it pays X' or 'if it pays X', this is a valid suspended emancipation. The child will be free only after paying. This is not void because the condition is on the child, making it a form of kitabah for the unborn.
This scenario from al-Zahiriyya shows a valid condition, unlike the one on the mother.
4
Examine the scope of wordings that trigger emancipation
The passage concludes by noting that various phrases – 'your pregnancy is free', 'what is in your womb is free', even 'the leech or embryo in your womb is free' – all effect emancipation. The ruling is broad and covers any stage of pregnancy.
This emphasizes the validity of emancipation regardless of the specific term used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق المصنف في عتق الحمل فشمل ما إذا أعتقه على مال فإنه يصح ولا يجب المال إذ لا وجه إلى إلزام المال على الجنين لعدم الولاية عليه ولا إلى إلزامه الأم لأنه في حق العتق نفس على حدة واشتراط بدل العتق على غير المعتق لا يجوز على ما مر في الخلع ؛ كذا في الهداية لكن لو أعتقه على مال على أمه لا بد من قبولها لعتقه وإن لم يلزمها شيء لما في المحيط : ولو قال أعتقت ما في بطنك على ألف عليك فقبلت فجاءت بولد لأقل من ستة أشهر يعتق بلا شيء لأن العتق معلق بقبول الأمة الألف وقد قبلت الألف فعتق الولد وبطل المال .اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : لو قال لأمته ما في بطنك حر متى أدى إلي ألفاً أو إذا أدى إلي ألفاً فوضعت لأقل من ستة أشهر فهو حر متى أدى إليه ألف درهم وأطلق في تحرير الحمل فشمل ما إذا قال حملك حر أو ما في بطنك حر أو قال العلقة أو المضغة التي في بطنك حر فإنه يعتق ما في بطنها؛ كذا في الخانية.
And the author (al-Musannif) spoke in general terms about the emancipation of the fetus, so it includes when he emancipates it in exchange for property, for it is valid and the property is not obligatory because there is no justification for imposing property on the fetus as there is no guardianship over it, nor for imposing it on the mother because in terms of emancipation it is a separate soul, and stipulating a price for emancipation on one who is not being emancipated is not permissible as passed in khul'. Thus in al-Hidaya. However, if he emancipates it in exchange for property on his mother, her acceptance of his emancipation is necessary even if nothing is binding on her, as in al-Muhit: and if he said 'I emancipate what is in your womb for one thousand on you' and she accepted, then she gave birth to a child at less than six months, it is free for nothing because the emancipation was suspended by the acceptance of the slave-girl of the thousand and she accepted the thousand, so the child is freed and the property is void. And in al-Zahiriyya: if he said to his slave-girl 'what is in your womb is free when it pays one thousand' or 'if it pays one thousand', and she gives birth at less than six months, it is free when it pays one thousand dirhams. And he spoke in general in the emancipation of the fetus, so it includes if he said 'your pregnancy is free' or 'what is in your womb is free' or he said 'the leech or the embryo in your womb is free', then what is in her womb is emancipated; thus in al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage covers the validity of emancipating a fetus in exchange for a payment. The author of the matn (al-Musannif) states that such an emancipation is valid, but the money is not due from the fetus because it cannot be held liable, nor from the mother because the fetus is a separate person in terms of emancipation. However, if the emancipation is conditioned on payment by the mother (e.g., 'on you'), then her acceptance is required, but still no money is due from her because the condition is void. Al-Muhit gives the example: if the master says 'I emancipate what is in your womb for one thousand on you' and the slave-girl accepts, then if she gives birth within six months, the child is free and the thousand is cancelled because the emancipation was contingent on acceptance, and the condition is invalid. Al-Zahiriyya adds a case: if the master says 'what is in your womb is free when it pays one thousand' or 'if it pays one thousand', then the child is free only upon payment (conditional emancipation). The passage also clarifies that various wordings (حملك حر, ما في بطنك حر, العلقة, المضغة) all effect emancipation of the fetus.

Key Terms
عتق على مال (itq 'ala mal): Emancipation of a slave in exchange for a payment, like kitabah; here applied to fetus
العلقة والمضغة (al-'alaqah wa al-mudghah): The leech (zygote) and the chewed lump (embryo) at early stages of pregnancy

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a master says to his pregnant slave-girl 'أعتقت ما في بطنك على ألف عليك' and she accepts?
A) The child is free and the thousand is due from the mother.
B) The child is free and the thousand is due from the child after birth.
C) The child is free, and the thousand is void because the condition is invalid.
D) The child remains a slave because the condition was not fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation of a fetus in exchange for payment is valid, but the payment is not enforceable on the fetus or the mother unless the condition is explicitly on the child and the child is capable of paying.

Formulations for Emancipating a Fetus and the Condition of Live Birth
Rules on emancipation wordings and the condition of live birth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different wordings for emancipating a fetus and identify when live birth is a condition according to Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Emancipation of a fetus is valid only after live birth; wordings vary but the condition of life is debated.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the legal effects of different formulations a master may use to emancipate an unborn child. It covers the determination of which twin is freed when the master says 'the oldest child,' and the conditional wording 'if you become pregnant.' A key dispute emerges: must the fetus be born alive for the emancipation to take effect? The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq does not require live birth, but al-Muhit suggests it is a condition, with implications for liability when a partner emancipates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Wordings
The passage discusses two wordings: 'the oldest child in your womb is free' and 'if you become pregnant with a boy it is free.' Each has specific legal rules.
2
Apply the Rule for Twins
When twins are born, the first to exit is considered the eldest and is the one freed under the first wording.
The firstborn is determined by actual exit order.
3
Determine the Effective Moment of Emancipation
For conditional wordings like 'if you become pregnant,' emancipation takes effect only at birth. If the mother is sold or the master dies before birth, the oath becomes void.
4
Analyze the Condition of Live Birth
There is a dispute: al-Musannif (author of Kanz) does not require live birth, but al-Muhit requires it. This affects liability in cases of stillbirth.
5
Apply the Dispute to a Case
If a partner emancipates the fetus and it is stillborn, no liability because life is uncertain. If born alive, the partner is liable (presumption of life at time of emancipation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال أكبر ولد في بطنك فهو حر فولدت ولدين في بطن فأولهما خروجاً أكبرهما وليس منه إن ولدت ولداً كذا في المحيط. وكذا لو قال إن حملت بولد فهو حر وهو حر : لأنه لا يعتق إلا بعد الولادة حتى لو باع الأم أو مات المولى قبل الولادة بطلت اليمين كما في البدائع . ولم يشترط المصنف ولادته حياً بعد عتقه وظاهر ما في المحيط أنه شرط قال : ولو أعتق أحد شريكي الأمة ما في بطنها فولدت توأماً ميتاً لا ضمان عليه لأن الإتلاف لم يثبت يقيناً لاحتمال أن الجنين لم يكن حياً ولم تنفخ فيه الروح أصلاً فلا يجب الضمان بالشك. ولو ولدت توأماً حياً يضمن لأن الظاهر أن الحياة كانت موجودة فيه وقت الإعتاق،
And if he said 'the oldest child in your womb is free' and she gives birth to two boys together, the first to come out is the eldest, and it is not from him [i.e. the condition applies to the last?] if she gave birth to one child? Thus in al-Muhit. And similarly if he said 'if you become pregnant with a boy it is free' and it is free: because it is not emancipated except after birth, to the extent that if he sells the mother or the master dies before birth, the oath is void as in al-Bada'i'. And the author (al-Musannif) did not require it to be born alive after its emancipation, and the apparent meaning of al-Muhit is that it is a condition. He said: and if one of two partners in a slave-girl emancipates what is in her womb, and she gives birth to twins dead, there is no liability because the destruction is not proven with certainty, as it is possible that the fetus was not alive and the soul had not been breathed into it at all, so liability is not due due to doubt. And if she gives birth to twins alive, he is liable because the apparent is that life existed in it at the time of emancipation.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage discusses various wordings for emancipating the fetus. First, if the master says 'أكبر ولد في بطنك فهو حر' (the oldest child in your womb is free) and she gives birth to twins, the first to emerge is considered the eldest and is the one freed. However, if she gives birth to only one child, the word 'أكبر' (oldest) is problematic; the text seems to say it is not from him? Possibly referring to a different ruling. Second, if the master says 'إن حملت بولد فهو حر' (if you become pregnant with a boy it is free), the emancipation takes effect only at birth, so if the mother is sold or the master dies before birth, the oath becomes void. The passage then addresses whether the fetus must be born alive for the emancipation to be valid. The author (al-Musannif) did not explicitly require live birth, but al-Muhit indicates it is a condition. In the case of partners: if one partner emancipates the fetus and the mother gives birth to stillborn twins, there is no liability because it is uncertain whether the fetus was alive at the time of emancipation (life begins when the soul is breathed in after 120 days). If born alive, the emancipating partner is liable because the presumption is that life existed.

Key Terms
أكبر ولد (akbar walad): The oldest child; in context of twins, the first to exit is the eldest
ولادته حياً (wiladatuhu hayy): Live birth as a condition for emancipation of fetus
إن حملت بولد (in hamaltu bi walad): Conditional wording 'if you become pregnant with a boy'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his pregnant slave-girl 'أكبر ولد في بطنك حر' and she gives birth to twins, which child is free?
A) Both children are free because the statement is general.
B) The first child to be born is free.
C) The last child to be born is free.
D) Neither is free because the wording is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation of a fetus is valid with various wordings, but it takes effect only at birth, and whether live birth is required is a point of ikhtilaf within the school.

Liability for Miscarriage After Partial Emancipation of Fetus: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on liability when one partner frees the fetus and an outsider strikes causing miscarriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the diyah and partner compensation when one co-owner frees a fetus and an outsider causes a miscarriage, and explain the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
If a partner frees the fetus, the striker pays diyah; Sahibayn say it's a free fetus's diyah and the partner must be compensated.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on a case involving a jointly-owned slave-girl. One partner emancipates the fetus, after which a third party strikes the mother, causing a miscarriage. The disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, centres on the status of the fetus at the time of the strike and the resulting liability. The analysis reveals how legal presumptions (zann) and the implications (illah) of a legal ruling shape divergent conclusions within the same school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Who emancipates? Who strikes? What is the outcome?
The fetus is jointly owned; one partner frees it; a stranger strikes the mother causing miscarriage.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
What diyah does the striker owe? Why? Is the emancipating partner liable to his co-owner?
Abu Hanifa: diyah of a slave fetus (half one-tenth of mother's value if male, one-tenth if female); no compensation to partner.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
What diyah do they impose? Why? Is the partner liable?
Sahibayn: diyah of a free fetus (ghurrah); emancipator compensates partner for half the fetus's value.
4
Analyze the key argument
How does each derive its ruling? What is the role of the striker's liability in each reasoning?
Abu Hanifa: partial emancipation makes it like a mukatab, still partly slave at strike. Sahibayn: striker's liability proves the fetus was alive and free from emancipator's share.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Which view is relied upon for fatwa and why?
Later Hanafi authorities prefer Sahibayn; the imposition of diyah implies life and freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أعتق أحد الشريكين الجنين فضرب أجنبي بطنها وألقت ميتاً فعلى الضارب نصف عشر قيمته إن كان غلاماً، وعشر قيمتها إن كانت جارية عند أبي حنيفة لأن معتق البعض كالمكاتب فهو حر ويتأخر إلقاء الجنين إلى تمامها أو أكثر بحيث يعلم أن ذلك الإلقاء من الضرب تأمل قوله: (وظاهر ما في المحيط أنه شرط الخ ) قال في النهر : للبحث فيه مجال. فعند الضرب صادفه وهو رقيق فيجب فيه ما يجب في جنين الأمة. وعندهما يجب فيه ما في جنين الحرة ويضمن المعتق نصفه لشريكه لأن الشرع لما أوجب ضمانه على الضارب فقد حكم بكونه حياً قبل الضرب فيكون المعتق بالإعتاق متلفاً نصيب شريكه فيضمن نصف قيمته، بذلك فيما أدى الضارب لأن المعتق ملك نصيب صاحبه بالضمان فإن الجنين مما يقبل ملك إلى ملك فإنه يملك بالوصية فصار نصيب صاحبه مكاتباً له، فهذا مكاتب وفاء فيقضى منه سعايته وما بقي فميراث لورثته أو لمعتقه لأنه مات حراً اهـ
And if one of the two partners emancipates the fetus, and a stranger strikes her belly and she miscarries a dead fetus, then according to Abu Hanifa, the striker owes half of one-tenth of its value if it is a boy, and one-tenth of its value if it is a girl, because the one who frees a part of a slave is like a mukatab, so it is free, and the miscarriage is delayed until completion [of six months?] or more such that one knows the miscarriage was from the blow. Ponder his statement. At the time of the blow, it was still a slave, so the due is what is due for the fetus of a slave-girl. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the due is what is due for the fetus of a free woman, and the emanicipator must compensate his partner for half its value because when the Law made the striker liable, it ruled that it was alive before the blow, so the emancipator, by the emancipation, destroyed his partner's share, so he guarantees half its value. Similarly, as to what the striker pays, because the emancipator owned his partner's share by the guarantee, for the fetus is capable of being owned from one owner to another, as it can be owned by will, so his partner's share becomes a mukatab for him. Thus this is a mukatab of wafa', and from it his 'sa'aya' (earning) is paid, and what remains is inheritance for his heirs or for his emancipator because he died free.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding a case where one partner in a jointly-owned slave-girl unilaterally emancipates the fetus she is carrying. Then a third party (ajnabi) strikes her, causing a miscarriage. The question is: what diyah (blood-money) is due from the striker, and is the emancipating partner liable to the other partner? Abu Hanifa's position: Since the emancipator only freed part of the fetus (its share of freedom), the fetus remains partly slave (like a mukatab). Therefore, at the moment of the strike, the fetus had some slave status, so the diyah is that of a slave's fetus: one-tenth of the mother's value if the fetus is female, half of one-tenth if male. The two disciples differ: They argue that once the Law imposes diyah on the striker (since the fetus died from the blow), it implies the fetus was alive and, due to the partial emancipation, it had attained freedom from the emancipator's share. Hence, the diyah should be that of a free fetus (which is a ghurrah: 500 dirhams or 5 camels). Additionally, the emancipator must compensate his partner for half the fetus's value because his act of emancipation destroyed the partner's share of the fetus. The partner's share becomes a 'mukatab of wafa' (a sort of contract) for the emancipator, and upon the fetus's death, its estate is used to pay its sa'aya (earnings or mukatabah payments) and the remainder is inherited by its heirs or the emancipator (as the one who freed it, since it died free).

Key Terms
معتق البعض (mu'tiq al-ba'd): One who emancipates a portion of a slave; treated like a mukatab according to Abu Hanifa
مكاتب وفاء (mukatab wafa'): A mukatab (slave with a contract of emancipation) that is like a trust; his earnings used to pay off and then inherited

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two disciples (Sahibayn), what is the diyah due from the striker who causes the fetus to miscarry after one partner has freed it?
A) Half of one-tenth of the mother's value if male, one-tenth if female.
B) The diyah of a free fetus, i.e. a ghurrah (500 dirhams or 5 camels).
C) No diyah because the fetus was partially slave.
D) Full diyah of a free person because it was fully free.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway: In a Hanafi intra-school dispute, the position that imposes diyah of a free fetus and partner compensation is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).
