Chapter: The slave who is partially freed. Whoever frees part of his slave does not free him completely, and he (the slave) must earn (to cover) the remainder, and he is like a mukatab. This is according to Abu Hanifa, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he becomes completely free. The scholars differed concerning the precise point of dispute. The author of al-Hidayah and many others held that it is based on whether emancipation is divisible according to him, so it is limited to what he freed, while according to them it is not divisible. Evidence was adduced from both sides. In Ghayat al-Bayan, the meaning of divisibility of emancipation and ownership is that the subject is divisible in accepting the ruling of emancipation, which is removal of ownership, such that it is removed from part but not from another part, and that the subject is divisible in accepting the ruling of ownership, such that part is owned by one and part by another. It does not mean that the essence of emancipation or ownership is divisible, for each is a single thing that does not accept division. In Fath al-Qadir: what consideration requires is that this is an error in determining the point of dispute, for they are not addressing a single point regarding divisibility and non-divisibility. The one who says emancipation is divisible does not mean it in the sense that the one who says it is not divisible means, i.e., the removal of slavery or its elimination, for there is no disagreement among them about its indivisibility; rather, the removal of ownership and its elimination, and there is no disagreement about its divisibility. So it should not be said that they differed over the divisibility of emancipation or the act of freeing, but rather the disagreement in reality is only about what the act of freeing necessitates primarily and essentially. According to him, it necessitates removal of ownership, and the removal of slavery follows from it, so the effect is necessarily divisible. However, the removal of slavery is only established upon the removal of ownership over the entirety, by way of a legal ruling, like the state of ritual impurity is not removed except by washing all limbs, and washing them is divisible. This is because emancipation is a legal force, which is the capacity for legal dispositions, and it is inconceivable that such capacity be established in a non-specified portion of him. So it is definitely ruled that there is no removal of ownership over the portion, and the removal of slavery depends on its divisibility. Ownership is divisible, so what we said about the removal of ownership over the remainder necessarily follows. Then evidence should be adduced from both sides that what is primarily established by it is removal of ownership or slavery, for that is the point of dispute. The reasoning (ma'na) is sound for Abu Hanifa: a person's disposal is confined to his right, and his right is ownership; as for slavery, it is the right of Allah or the public. As for the transmitted proof, it is what is in the two Sahihs, raised to the Prophet: 'Whoever frees his share in a slave and has wealth sufficient to cover the value of the slave, he shall be assessed a fair value...' Then after the hadith, the commentary continues discussing the assessment, the ruling of badi', the reconciliation of statements, and the details of the slave's status as a mukatab, including differences in cases of homicide and sales. The passage ends with references to hadith sources.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq on the chapter of partial emancipation from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The author begins by stating the ruling: if someone frees part of his slave, the slave does not become entirely free; instead, he must earn (saa'a) the remaining portion of his value, and he is treated like a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom). This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the slave becomes completely free. The commentator then delves into the precise nature of this disagreement. He notes that scholars differed in framing the dispute: some, like the author of al-Hidayah, say it hinges on whether the act of emancipation is divisible (tajazzu). According to Abu Hanifa, emancipation is divisible, so only the freed portion is affected; according to the Sahibayn, it is indivisible, so the entire slave becomes free. The commentator critiques this framing, citing Fath al-Qadir, which argues that the real disagreement is not about divisibility of the act itself (which is a single entity) but about what the act primarily necessitates: for Abu Hanifa, it is the removal of ownership (which is divisible), and the removal of slavery follows indirectly; for the Sahibayn, it is the direct removal of slavery (which is indivisible). The commentator then provides Abu Hanifa's rational proof: a person's disposal is limited to his own right (ownership), while slavery is a right of Allah or the public, so the act cannot directly affect slavery. The transmitted proof is the hadith: 'Whoever frees his share in a slave...' which implies that if the owner has enough wealth, the slave is fully freed by force of law, but if not, only the freed part is free. The commentary continues with a reconciliation of the various scholarly statements, emphasizing that despite the dispute, the operative ruling is that the slave becomes a mustas'a (one who must earn) similar to a mukatab, with specific differences in cases of inability to pay, homicide, and sale. The passage is dense and aims to clarify the legal reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): The act of emancipating a slave; in this context, discussing its divisibility.
سعاية (sa'ayah): The slave's effort to earn the remaining value of himself after partial emancipation.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
تجزؤ (tajazzu'): Divisibility; the concept of whether the act of emancipation can affect only part of the slave.
رق (riqq): Slavery; the legal status of being owned as a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens when an owner frees part of his slave?
A) The slave becomes completely free immediately.
B) The slave remains completely enslaved, and the freeing is void.
C) Only the freed portion becomes free, and the slave must earn the remaining value.
D) The slave becomes free only if the owner pays compensation to the slave.

Key Takeaway
Partial emancipation results in the slave becoming a mustas'a, not fully free, due to the distinction between ownership (divisible) and slavery (a right of Allah).

Partial Emancipation: Silent Partner's Options
Explains the rulings when one co-owner emancipates his share of a slave, focusing on the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three options available to a silent co-owner under Abu Hanifa when a co-owner frees his share, and explain how the Sahibayn restrict these options, citing the underlying usuli difference on tajazzu' al-i'taq.

Core Idea
Emancipation is divisible according to Abu Hanifa; the silent partner retains ownership and has choices.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the case where one co-owner of a slave frees his share. The key question: what legal options does the silent partner have? Abu Hanifa grants three choices: to complete the emancipation, to seek earnings from the slave (istis'a'), or to claim damages from a wealthy emancipator. The Sahibayn, however, restrict this to only damages or seeking earnings. The dispute hinges on whether emancipation is divisible (tajazzu') — a fundamental usuli difference within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling in the matn
Identify the initial ruling presented: the silent partner has options to emancipate, seek earnings, or claim damages. This is the base position.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqaiq.
2
Identify the two positions
Distinguish between Abu Hanifa's view (three options) and the Sahibayn's view (only damages or seeking earnings). The Sahibayn do not allow the option to emancipate the remaining share.
3
Trace the usuli foundations
The dispute rests on two principles: (1) tajazzu' al-i'taq (divisibility of emancipation) and (2) whether the emancipator's wealth prevents istis'a'. Abu Hanifa holds emancipation is divisible; Sahibayn hold it is indivisible.
The hadith cited is used to support both sides.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
Abu Hanifa analogizes to a garment blown into another's dye: the owner must pay for the dye regardless of wealth. Similarly, the slave retains the value, so the emancipator must compensate; if the slave is poor, istis'a' applies.
This qiyas supports that wealth does not prevent istis'a'.
5
Note the practical differences (thamarah)
Under Abu Hanifa, the silent partner can free the entire slave immediately; under Sahibayn, if the emancipator is poor, the silent partner must wait for the slave to earn the value of the share.
Also, additional options like tadbir and kitabah are discussed but essentially reduce to istis'a'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتق باب العبد يعتق بعضه
قوله : ( وإن أعتق نصيبه فلشريكه أن يحرر أو يستسعي والولاء لهما أو يضمن له موسراً ويرجع به على العبد والولاء له وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : ليس له إلا لضمان مع والسعاية مع الإعسار ولا المعتق على العبد وهذه المسألة تنبني على أصلين: أحدهما تجزؤ الإعتاق وعدمه على ما بيناه والثاني أن يسار المعتق لا يمنع استسعاء العبد عنده، وعندهما يمنع لهما في الثاني قوله عليه السلام في الرجل يعتق نصيبه إن كان غنياً ضمن وإن كان فقيراً سعى في حصة الآخر قسم والقسمة تنافي الشركة وله أنه إن احتبست مالية نصيبه عند العبد فله أن يضمنه كما إذا هبت الريح بثوب إنسان وألقته في صبغ غيره حتى انصبغ به فعلى صاحب الثوب قيمة صبغ الآخر، موسراً كان أو معسراً لما قلنا، فكذا هنا إلا أن العبد فقير فيستسعيه. وإنما ثبت الخيار للشريك الساكت لقيام ملكه في الباقي إذ الأعناق يتجزا عنده، وقد ذكر المصنف أن له الإعتاق والاستسعاء والتضمين وزاد عليه في التحفة خيارين آخرين : التدبير والكتابة. وإنما تركهما المصنف لأن الكتابة ترجع إلى معنى الاستسعاء ولو عجز استسعى، ولو امتنع العبد من السعاية يؤاجره جبراً ويدل على أن الكتابة في معنى الاستسعاء أنه لو كاتبه على أكثر من قيمته إن كان من النقدين لا يجوز إلا أن يكون قدراً يتغابن الناس فيه لأن الشرع أوجب السعاية على قيمته فلا يجوز الأكثر، وكذا لو كان صالحه على عرض أكثر من قيمته جاز، وإن كاتبه على حيوان جازت وأما التدبير ففي البدائع والمحيط فإن اختار التدبير فدبر نصيبه صار نصيبه مدبراً عند أبي حنيفة لأن نصيبه باق على ملكه فيحتمل التخريج إلى العتق والتدبير تخريج له إلى العتق إلا أنه لا يجوز له أن يتركه على حاله ليعتق بعد الموت بل تجب عليه السعاية للحال فيؤدي فيعتق لأن تدبيره اختيار منه للسعاية اهـ. فلما كان التدبير والكتابة راجعين إلى السعاية لم يذكرهما المصنف، وظاهر كلام الكمال أنه لا فائدة لهما حيث يرجعان إليها قلت بل لهما فائدة؛ أما في التدبير فلأن الشريك المدبر إذا مات عتق العبد كله بسبب التدبير وسقطت عنه السعاية إذا كان يخرج من ثلث ماله ولولا التدبير لسعى للورثة كالمكاتب وأما في الكتابة فلأن فائدتها تعيين البدل لأنه لولا الكتابة لاحتيج إلى تقويمه وإيجاب نصف القيمة، وقد يحتاج فيها إلى القضاء عند التنازع
Chapter: The slave partially emancipated. His statement: (And if he emancipated his share, then his partner has the option to emancipate or to seek earnings (istis'a') and the patronage (wala') belongs to both of them, or he may claim damages from him if he is wealthy and then claim it back from the slave, and the patronage belongs to him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He only has the option of claiming damages with wealth and seeking earnings with insolvency, and the one who emancipates has nothing on the slave. And this issue is based on two principles: one is the divisibility of emancipation and its opposite as we explained, and the second is that the wealth of the emancipator does not prevent seeking earnings from the slave according to him, but according to them it does prevent. According to the second, the Prophet ﷺ said concerning a man who emancipates his share: 'If he is wealthy, he must pay damages; if he is poor, the slave seeks earnings for the share of the other.' Division negates partnership. And his proof: If the financial value of his share is retained with the slave, he has the right to claim damages, just as when the wind blows a garment of a person and throws it into the dye of another until it gets dyed with it, then the owner of the garment owes the value of the dye of the other, whether he is wealthy or poor, for what we said. So here too, except that the slave is poor, so he seeks earnings from him. The option is established for the silent partner because his ownership remains in the remaining part, since emancipation is divisible according to him. The author mentioned that he has the options of emancipation, seeking earnings, and claiming damages. In al-Tuhfah, two more options were added: tadbir (making mudabbar) and kitabah (making a contract of kitabah). The author omitted them because kitabah reverts to the meaning of seeking earnings, and if he becomes unable, he seeks earnings; if the slave refuses to seek earnings, he hires him out by force. And it indicates that kitabah is in the meaning of seeking earnings that if he makes a kitabah contract for more than his value, if it is in currency it is not permissible unless it is an amount people tolerate, because the law obligated seeking earnings for his value, so more is not permissible. Similarly, if he makes a compromise for a commodity more than its value, it is permissible, and if he makes a kitabah contract for an animal, it is permissible. As for tadbir, in al-Bada'i' and al-Muhit: If he chooses tadbir and makes his share mudabbar, his share becomes mudabbar according to Abu Hanifa, because his share remains in his ownership, so it can be directed towards emancipation; tadbir is a direction towards emancipation, except that he is not allowed to leave him as he is to become free after death, rather he must seek earnings immediately, so he pays and becomes free, because his tadbir is a choice of seeking earnings. So since tadbir and kitabah revert to seeking earnings, the author did not mention them. The apparent statement of al-Kamal is that there is no benefit in them since they revert to it. I say: Rather there is benefit in them. As for tadbir, because if the mudabbar partner dies, the entire slave becomes free due to tadbir, and the seeking earnings drops from him if it comes out of one-third of his wealth; without tadbir, he would seek earnings for the heirs like the mukatab. As for kitabah, its benefit is specifying the substitute, because without kitabah, there would be a need to appraise him and impose half the value, and there may be need for judicial settlement in case of dispute.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the case where one co-owner of a slave frees his share. It presents the ruling from the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) and then explains the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. According to Abu Hanifa, the silent partner (the one who did not free) has three options: (1) complete the emancipation of the remainder (by paying the share of the other?), (2) seek earnings from the slave (istis'a') to pay the value of the remaining share, or (3) if the emancipating partner is wealthy, claim damages from him for the value of the share and then the emancipator can recover that from the slave. According to the Sahibayn, the silent partner only has the option of claiming damages from a wealthy emancipator or seeking earnings from a poor one; they do not allow the option of completing the emancipation if the emancipator is poor. The passage traces the ikhtilaf to two usul: (1) whether emancipation is divisible (tajazzu'), and (2) whether the wealth of the emancipator prevents seeking earnings. Abu Hanifa holds that emancipation is divisible, so the share freed is free, and the remaining share stays owned. The Sahibayn argue that emancipation is indivisible, so partial freeing triggers full freedom with a financial obligation. The hadith of the Prophet is cited: 'If the man was wealthy, he pays damages; if poor, the slave seeks earnings for the share of the other.' The author then defends Abu Hanifa's position with a qiyas (analogy) of wind blowing a garment into another's dye: the garment owner must pay for the dye whether rich or poor, because the value is retained. Here, the slave retains the value of the freed share, so the emancipator must compensate, but if the slave is poor, seeking earnings is appropriate. The passage also clarifies that the silent partner's option is based on the existence of his ownership in the remaining part. Additional options like tadbir and kitabah are discussed, but the author (Ibn Nujaym) explains that they essentially lead to seeking earnings. However, he notes benefits: with tadbir, if the partner dies, the slave becomes free from the one-third, potentially eliminating the need for seeking earnings; with kitabah, the substitute is fixed, avoiding appraisal disputes.

Key Terms
استسعاء (istis'a'): Seeking earnings from the slave to pay the value of the remaining share; the slave is hired out or allowed to work to pay off the debt.
تضمين (tadmeen): Claiming damages from the wealthy emancipator for the value of the silent partner's share; the emancipator must pay and can recover from the slave.
تجزؤ العتق (tajazzu' al-i'taq): The concept that emancipation can be divided into parts; freeing a share of a slave results in that share becoming free while the rest remains owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what options does the silent co-owner have when the other co-owner frees his share of a slave?
A) Emancipate the remaining share, seek earnings, or claim damages from the emancipator if wealthy.
B) Only claim damages from the emancipator regardless of wealth.
C) Only seek earnings from the slave.
D) Only accept the slave becoming free without any obligation.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa grants the silent partner three options due to the divisibility of emancipation; the Sahibayn restrict options based on indivisibility.

Options of the Silent Partner When a Co-Owner Frees His Share
Commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqā'iq regarding partial manumission and options of the silent partner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three options available to the silent partner under Abu Hanifa, explain why the Sahibayn limit them to guarantee or earnings, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When one co-owner frees his share, the silent partner retains the right to free his share, seek earnings, or demand a guarantee – but the Sahibayn limit this to guarantee or earnings only.

The classical Hanafi texts discuss a scenario where two people jointly own a slave, and one partner frees his share. What rights does the silent partner have? This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether the silent partner can also free his share or is confined to financial remedies. We will examine the proofs, the underlying principle of divisibility of manumission, and the practical consequences for the slave's freedom and guardianship (wala').

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify that Abu Hanifa holds the silent partner has three options: free his own share, seek earnings (istis'a') from the slave, or demand a guarantee (damān) from the manumitter if wealthy.
This is the default ruling in the matn of Kanz al-Daqā'iq.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the silent partner cannot free his share; he can only demand a guarantee if the manumitter is wealthy or seek earnings if poor.
Their view is based on the hadith interpretation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn cite the hadith: 'If he is wealthy, he guarantees; if poor, the slave seeks earnings for the other's share.' They interpret this as a division (qisma) that negates partnership, implying manumission is not divisible.
Division here means the slave's value is split, not the manumission act itself.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa uses an analogy (qiyas): just as the owner of a garment blown into another's dye must pay for the dye regardless of wealth, so here the manumitter retains the value of the silent partner's share and can be made liable. This analogy supports the divisibility of manumission.
The analogy shows that liability for value does not depend on wealth, so the silent partner's share remains his property to free.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the silent partner can free his share, making the slave fully free and wala' shared; under the Sahibayn, the slave remains partially free and partially owned, with wala' solely for the first manumitter.
The mufta bihi position is Abu Hanifa's view, as reflected in the matn and commentary.

كتاب العتق باب العبد يعتق بعضه نصيبه فلشريكه أن يحرر أو يستسعي والولاء لهما أو يضمن له موسراً ويرجع به على العبد والولاء له ولو شهد كل بعتق نصيب صاحبه سعى لهما ولو علق أحدهما عتقه كالجمع بين العبد المعين والمبهم ولزمه بيانه. وفي جوامع الفقه : الاستسعاء أن يؤاجره ويأخذ قيمة ما بقي من أجره قالوا وعلى هذا الخلاف التدبير والاستيلاد . قوله : ( وإن أعتق نصيبه فلشريكه أن يحرر أو يستسعي والولاء لهما أو على ما بيناه والثاني أن يسار المعتق لا يمنع استسعاء العبد عنده، وعندهما يمنع لهما في الثاني قوله عليه السلام في الرجل يعتق نصيبه إن كان غنياً ضمن وإن كان فقيراً سعى في حصة الآخر قسم والقسمة تنافي العبد فقير فيستسعيه. وإنما ثبت الخيار للشريك الساكت لقيام ملكه في الباقي إذ الأعناق يتجزا عنده، وقد ذكر المصنف أن له الإعتاق والاستسعاء والتضمين وزاد عليه في التحفة خيارين آخرين : التدبير والكتابة. وإنما تركهما المصنف لأن الكتابة ترجع إلى معنى الاستسعاء ولو عجز استسعى، ولو امتنع العبد من السعاية يؤاجره جبراً ويدل على أن الكتابة في معنى الاستسعاء أنه لو كاتبه على أكثر من قيمته إن كان من النقدين لا يجوز إلا أن يكون قدراً يتغابن الناس فيه لأن الشرع أوجب السعاية على قيمته فلا يجوز الأكثر، وكذا لو كان صالحه على عرض أكثر من قيمته جاز، وإن كاتبه على حيوان جازت وأما التدبير ففي البدائع والمحيط فإن اختار التدبير فدبر نصيبه صار نصيبه مدبراً عند أبي حنيفة لأن نصيبه باق على ملكه فيحتمل التخريج إلى العتق والتدبير تخريج له إلى العتق إلا أنه لا يجوز له أن يتركه على حاله ليعتق بعد الموت بل تجب عليه السعاية للحال فيؤدي فيعتق لأن تدبيره اختيار منه للسعاية اهـ. فلما كان التدبير والكتابة راجعين إلى السعاية لم يذكرهما المصنف، وظاهر كلام الكمال أنه لا فائدة لهما حيث يرجعان إليها قلت بل لهما فائدة؛ أما في التدبير فلأن الشريك المدبر إذا مات عتق العبد كله بسبب التدبير وسقطت عنه السعاية إذا كان يخرج من ثلث ماله ولولا التدبير لسعى للورثة كالمكاتب وأما في الكتابة فلأن فائدتها تعيين البدل لأنه لولا الكتابة لاحتيج إلى تقويمه وإيجاب نصف القيمة، وقد يحتاج فيها إلى القضاء عند التنازع في المقدار ولا يدل عدم جواز الكتابة على أكثر من القيمة زيادة فاحشة على أنه لا فائدة لها لأن الحكم كذلك في صلح الساكت مع الشريك المعتق . قال في البدائع : ولو صالح الذي لم يعتق العبد المعتق على مال فإن هذا لا يخلو من الأقسام التي ذكرناها في المكاتبة فإن كان الصلح على الدراهم والدنانير على نصف قيمته فهو جائز، وكذا إذا كان على أقل من نصف قيمته، وكذا إذا صالح على أكثر من نصف القيمة مما يتغابن الناس في مثله فأما إذا كان على أكثر من قيمته مما لا يتغابن الناس في مثله فالفضل باطل في قولهم جميعاً لأنه ربا .اهـ. فالحق أن الخيارات خمسة كما هو في البدائع وغيرها، وأطلق المصنف في تحرير الشريك فشمل العتق منجزاً ومضافاً. قال في فتح القدير : وينبغي إذا أضافه أن لا تقبل منه إضافته إلى زمان طويل لأنه كالتدبير معنى ولو دبره وجب عليه السعاية في الحال فيعتق كما صرحوا به فينبغي أن يضاف إلى مدة تشاكل مدة الاستسعاء اهـ. وأشار المصنف بذكر هذه الخيارات إلى أنه ليس له خيار الترك على حاله لأنه لا سبيل إلى الانتفاع به مع ثبوت الحرية في جزء منه فلا بد من تخريجه إلى العتق كما في البدائع، وإلى أنه لو اختار واحداً مما ذكر تعين فإن اختار الاستسعاء فليس له التضمين وعكسه، نعم إذا اختار الاستسعاء فله الإعتاق وإلى أنه ليس للساكت أن يختار التضمين في البعض والسعاية في البعض كما في المبسوط وأطلق في تضمين الموسر وهو مقيد بأن يكون الإعتاق بغير إذنه فلو أعتق أحدهما نصيبه بإذن صاحبه فلا ضمان عليه وإنما الاستسعاء في ظاهر الرواية. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يضمن لأنه عنده ضمان تملك لا إتلاف ولذا كان كل الولاء له وضمان التملك لا يسقط بالرضا. وجه ظاهر الرواية أن ضمان الإعتاق ضمان إتلاف ولذا يختلف باليسار والإعسار، وإنما ملك نصيب صاحبه بمقتضى الإعتاق تصحيحاً له لا قصداً لأن الإعتاق وضع لإبطال الملك فثبوت الملك بما وضع لإبطاله يكون تناقضاً والمقتضى تبع للمقتضى فكان حكمه حكم المقتضى والمقتضى وهو الإعتاق لا يوجب فلذا تبعه؛ كذا في المحيط. ولو كان الساكت جماعة فاختار بعضهم السعاية وبعضهم فلكل منهم ما اختار في قول أبي حنيفة ؛ كذا في البدائع واختلف في حد اليسار هنا ففي الهداية، ثم المعتبر يسار التيسير وهو أن يملك من المال قدر نصيب الآخر لا يسار الغني لأن به يقيد له النظر من الجانبين بتحقيق ما قصده المعتق من القربة وإيصال بدل حق الساكت إليه. وجعله في فتح القدير ظاهر الرواية قال : وفي رواية الحسن استثنى الكفاف وهو المنزل والخادم وثياب البدن والذي يظهر أن استثناء الكفاف لا بد منه على ظاهر الرواية ولذا اقتصر عليه في المحيط فقال : ثم حد اليسار أن يكون المعتق مالكاً لمقدار قيمة ما بقي من العبد سوى ملبوسه وقوت يومه لا ما يعتبر في حرمة الصدقة وصححه في المجتبى. وتعتبر قيمة العبد في الضمان والسعاية يوم الإعتاق لأنه سبب الضمان كالغصب، وكذلك يعتبر الضمان مع الرضا الضمان
Chapter of Manumission: Section on the slave who is partially freed. His share: so his partner may free or seek earnings (istis'a') and the guardianship (wala') is for both of them, or he may demand a guarantee (daman) from him if he is wealthy and then recover it from the slave, and the guardianship is for him. And if each testified that the other freed his share, then both seek earnings for both. And if one of them suspended his emancipation, it is like combining a slave and a free person, so it becomes invalid for both because the kitaba of a partially freed slave cannot be rescinded, unlike the mukatab. So these are three issues in which a partially freed slave differs from a mukatab. And they did not mention them explicitly because they are two effects of the inability to rescind, as is not hidden. And he made absolute 'some' so it includes the specific and the ambiguous, and it was necessary for him to clarify it. In Jami' al-Fiqh: istis'a' is that he hires him out and takes the value of what remains from his hire. They said: on this is based the disagreement regarding tadbir and istilad. His statement: (And if he frees his share, his partner may free or seek earnings and the wala' is for both, or he may demand a guarantee from him if he is wealthy and then recover it from the slave, and the wala' is for him) — this is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He only has the guarantee with wealth and seeking earnings with poverty, and he does not free the slave. This issue is built on two principles: one is the divisibility of manumission or not as we explained, and the second is that the wealth of the manumitter does not prevent the seeking of earnings from the slave according to him (Abu Hanifa), but according to them it prevents. In support of them (the Sahibayn) is his statement (peace be upon him) regarding the man who frees his share: if he is wealthy he guarantees, and if he is poor the slave seeks earnings for the other's share. And division (qisma) negates partnership. And for Abu Hanifa is that if the wealth of his share is retained with the slave, then he may hold him liable, just as when the wind blows a person's garment and throws it into another's dye until it becomes dyed, then the owner of the garment must pay the value of the other's dye, whether the owner is wealthy or poor for what we said. So here too, except that the slave is poor so he seeks earnings from him. And the choice is established for the silent partner because his ownership remains in the remaining share, since necks are divisible according to him. And the author mentioned that he has freeing, seeking earnings, and imposing a guarantee, and in al-Tuhfa he added two other options: tadbir and kitaba. The author omitted them because kitaba returns to the meaning of istis'a': if he is unable, he seeks earnings, and if the slave refuses to seek earnings, he hires him out by force. And it indicates that kitaba is in the meaning of istis'a' that if he made a kitaba with him for more than his value, if it is from the two coin types, it is not permissible unless it is an amount that people overlook, because the law obligated seeking earnings for his value, so more is not permissible. Similarly, if he made a settlement with him for goods more than his value, it is permissible, and if he made a kitaba with him for an animal, it is permissible. As for tadbir, in al-Bada'i' and al-Muhit: if he chooses tadbir and makes his share mudabbar, his share becomes mudabbar according to Abu Hanifa because his share remains in his ownership, so it can be extended to manumission, and tadbir extends it to manumission, except that he is not allowed to leave it as is to become free after death, rather he must seek earnings immediately, so he pays and becomes free because his tadbir is his choice of seeking earnings. So since tadbir and kitaba revert to seeking earnings, the author did not mention them. And the apparent statement of al-Kamal is that there is no benefit in them since they revert to it. I say: rather, there is benefit in them. As for tadbir, because the mudabbar partner, if he dies, the whole slave becomes free due to the tadbir and seeking earnings is dropped if it comes out of one-third of his wealth; without tadbir he would seek earnings for the heirs like the mukatab. As for kitaba, its benefit is fixing the substitute because without kitaba one would need to evaluate and impose half the value, and one may need recourse to the judge in case of dispute about the amount. And the fact that kitaba for more than the value with a gross excess is not permissible does not indicate that there is no benefit, because the ruling is the same in the settlement of the silent partner with the manumitting partner. He said in al-Bada'i': And if the one who did not manumit makes a settlement with the manumitting partner for property, this is not free from the categories we mentioned in kitaba. If the settlement is for dirhams and dinars for half his value, it is permissible; similarly if it is for less than half his value; similarly if he settled for more than half the value by an amount that people overlook. But if it is for more than his value by an amount that people do not overlook, the excess is invalid according to all of them because it is riba. So the correct view is that there are five options, as in al-Bada'i' and others. The author made absolute 'the partner' so it includes immediate and delayed emancipation. He said in Fath al-Qadir: It is appropriate that if he adds it, his addition to a long time should not be accepted because it is like tadbir in meaning, and if he makes it mudabbar, he must seek earnings immediately so he becomes free, as they explicitly stated; therefore it should be added for a period similar to the period of seeking earnings. And the author indicated by mentioning these options that he does not have the option to leave it as is, because there is no way to benefit from it while freedom is established in a part, so it must be extended to manumission as in al-Bada'i'. And that if he chooses one of the mentioned options, it becomes fixed: if he chooses seeking earnings, he does not have the guarantee, and vice versa. Yes, if he chooses seeking earnings, he may free. And that it is not allowed for the silent partner to choose the guarantee for part and seeking earnings for part, as in al-Mabsut. And he made absolute the guarantee of the wealthy, and it is restricted to the case where the manumission is without permission. So if one of them freed his share with the permission of his partner, there is no guarantee, rather seeking earnings, according to the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf that he guarantees because according to him it is a guarantee of ownership not destruction, so all the wala' is for him, and the guarantee of ownership is not dropped by consent. The justification of the apparent narration is that the guarantee of manumission is a guarantee of destruction, hence it varies with wealth and poverty; he only owned the share of his partner by virtue of manumission to validate it, not intentionally, because manumission is established to nullify ownership, so establishing ownership by that which is established to nullify it is contradictory, and the consequence follows the cause, so its ruling is the ruling of the cause, and the cause which is manumission does not cause it, so it follows it. Likewise in al-Muhit. And if the silent partner is a group, then some choose seeking earnings and others choose [another option], each has what he chose according to Abu Hanifa; likewise in al-Bada'i'. And they differed about the definition of wealth here: in al-Hidaya, the considered wealth is that of facilitation: that he possesses of property the amount of the other's share, not the wealth of the rich, because thereby his consideration is bound from both sides to achieve the intent of the manumitter for closeness and to deliver the substitute for the silent partner's right. And he made it in Fath al-Qadir the apparent narration. He said: In the narration of al-Hasan, he excluded basic necessities: the house, the servant, and the clothing of the body. And what appears is that the exclusion of basic necessities is necessary according to the apparent narration, hence he limited it to that in al-Muhit, saying: Then the definition of wealth is that the manumitter possesses the amount of the value of what remains of the slave apart from his clothing and his sustenance for the day, not what is considered for the prohibition of charity, and he corrected it in al-Mujtaba. And the value of the slave is considered for the guarantee and seeking earnings on the day of manumission because it is the cause of the guarantee, like usurpation; similarly, the guarantee with consent is considered.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq explaining the ruling when a co-owner (sharīk) frees his share of a jointly-owned slave. The core ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqā'iq states that the silent partner (al-sākit) has three options: (1) to free his own share, (2) to seek earnings (istis'ā') from the slave to pay for the remaining share, or (3) to demand a guarantee (damān) from the manumitting partner if that partner is wealthy, and then recover it from the slave. The author notes the intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Hanīfa holds that all three options are available because manumission is divisible (yatajazzā), so the silent partner's share remains his property. The two disciples (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad al-Shaybānī) hold that the silent partner does NOT have the option to free his share; he only has the right to guarantee from a wealthy manumitter or seek earnings from the slave if the manumitter is poor. Their evidence is the hadith: 'If he is wealthy, he guarantees; if poor, the slave seeks earnings for the other's share,' which they interpret as a division (qisma) that negates partnership. Abū Hanīfa responds with an analogy: just as when a garment is blown into another's dye, the owner must pay the value of the dye whether rich or poor, so here the manumitter retains the value of the slave's share (since the slave is not destroyed), so he can be made liable. The commentator then discusses additional options: tadbīr (postponed emancipation) and kitāba (contract of manumission). Some scholars include these as options, but the author of the matn omitted them because they essentially revert to istis'ā'. However, the commentator argues they have distinct benefits: tadbīr allows the slave to be freed from the manumitter's estate, dropping the obligation of istis'ā', while kitāba fixes the amount to be paid. The passage also addresses the definition of yasār (wealth): it is the ability to pay the remaining share after deducting basic necessities like clothing and daily food. This is stricter than the threshold for zakāt. The value of the slave is assessed on the day of manumission. The silent partner's choice is binding; if he chooses one option, he cannot switch to another. If the manumission was done with the silent partner's permission, there is no guarantee; instead, istis'ā' is required according to the apparent narration (zāhir al-riwāya). There is also a sub-issue if there are multiple silent partners: each can independently choose their option according to Abū Hanīfa.

Key Terms
استسعاء (istis'ā'): The process where the slave is required to work and earn money to pay the value of the remaining share to the silent partner.
تضمين (tadmiīn): Making the manumitting co-owner liable to pay the value of the silent partner's share, if the manumitter is wealthy.
يسار (yasār): Wealth sufficient to pay the value of the remaining share of the slave, after deducting basic necessities like clothing and daily sustenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad regarding the options of the silent partner when the co-owner frees his share?
A) The silent partner may free his share, seek earnings, or demand a guarantee.
B) The silent partner may only demand a guarantee if the manumitter is wealthy, or seek earnings if poor; he cannot free his share.
C) The silent partner must free his share immediately.
D) The silent partner has no options; the slave is automatically freed.

Key Takeaway
The core dispute in partial manumission is whether manumission is divisible (Abu Hanifa) or indivisible (Sahibayn), which determines whether the silent partner can free his own share or only seek financial redress.

intermediate
12 min
The Nature of Dhaman in Partial Emancipation: Abu Hanifa vs Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dhaman and options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi views on the nature of dhaman (itlaf vs tamalluk) and explain how each view affects the options of the silent partner and the distribution of wala'.

Core Idea
Dhaman in partial emancipation is a guarantee of destruction, not ownership — therefore it depends on the co-owner's wealth.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: when one co-owner frees his share of a joint slave without the other's permission, what is the legal nature of the compensation (dhaman) owed to the silent partner? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ on whether the dhaman is for destruction (itlaf) or ownership (tamalluk). This distinction affects the silent partner's options, the co-owner's liability, and the allocation of wala'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the case: a jointly-owned slave is partially freed by one co-owner without the other's permission. What are the possible courses of action for the silent partner?
2
List the Options of the Silent Partner
Based on the passage, enumerate the options: the silent partner can free his own share (i'taq), demand compensation from the co-owner (tadmin), or have the slave work to earn his freedom (sa'yah). The choice is binding once made.
These are derived from al-Bada'i and other sources.
3
Understand the Two Views on Dhaman
Contrast Abu Hanifa's view (dhaman itlaf - guarantee of destruction) with Abu Yusuf's view (dhaman tamalluk - guarantee of ownership). Note how each view defines the nature of the co-owner's liability.
The passage explains that under Abu Hanifa, the liability varies with wealth; under Abu Yusuf, it is fixed and not dropped by consent.
4
Trace the Practical Consequences
Apply each view to determine: (a) when tadmin is available (only if co-owner is wealthy under Abu Hanifa; always under Abu Yusuf), (b) who gets the wala' (shared under Abu Hanifa; all to the freeing co-owner under Abu Yusuf), and (c) the effect of the silent partner's consent.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is crucial for fatwa.
5
Examine the Qiyas to Ghasb
The passage analogizes the co-owner's liability to that of a usurper (ghasb). Analyze how this qiyas supports Abu Hanifa's view: the dhaman is for the destruction of the partner's property, similar to usurpation.
Abu Yusuf objects that emancipation is not destruction but acquisition. The Hanafi response is that compensation is for the loss, not ownership transfer.

كتاب العتق باب العبد يعتق بعضه
في المقدار ولا يدل عدم جواز الكتابة على أكثر من القيمة زيادة فاحشة على أنه لا فائدة لها لأن الحكم كذلك في صلح الساكت مع الشريك المعتق .
قال في البدائع : ولو صالح الذي لم يعتق العبد المعتق على مال فإن هذا لا يخلو من الأقسام التي ذكرناها في المكاتبة فإن كان الصلح على الدراهم والدنانير على نصف قيمته فهو جائز، وكذا إذا كان على أقل من نصف قيمته، وكذا إذا صالح على أكثر من نصف القيمة مما يتغابن الناس في مثله فأما إذا كان على أكثر من قيمته مما لا يتغابن الناس في مثله فالفضل باطل في قولهم جميعاً لأنه ربا .اهـ. فالحق أن الخيارات خمسة كما هو في البدائع وغيرها، وأطلق المصنف في تحرير الشريك فشمل العتق منجزاً ومضافاً. قال في فتح القدير : وينبغي إذا أضافه أن لا تقبل منه إضافته إلى زمان طويل لأنه كالتدبير معنى ولو دبره وجب عليه السعاية في الحال فيعتق كما صرحوا به فينبغي أن يضاف إلى مدة تشاكل مدة الاستسعاء اهـ. وأشار المصنف بذكر هذه الخيارات إلى أنه ليس له خيار الترك على حاله لأنه لا سبيل إلى الانتفاع به مع ثبوت الحرية في جزء منه فلا بد من تخريجه إلى العتق كما في البدائع، وإلى أنه لو اختار واحداً مما ذكر تعين فإن اختار الاستسعاء فليس له التضمين وعكسه، نعم إذا اختار الاستسعاء فله الإعتاق وإلى أنه ليس للساكت أن يختار التضمين في البعض والسعاية في البعض كما في المبسوط وأطلق في تضمين الموسر وهو مقيد بأن يكون الإعتاق بغير إذنه فلو أعتق أحدهما نصيبه بإذن صاحبه فلا ضمان عليه وإنما الاستسعاء في ظاهر الرواية. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يضمن لأنه عنده ضمان تملك لا إتلاف ولذا كان كل الولاء له وضمان التملك لا يسقط بالرضا. وجه ظاهر الرواية أن ضمان الإعتاق ضمان إتلاف ولذا يختلف باليسار والإعسار، وإنما ملك نصيب صاحبه بمقتضى الإعتاق تصحيحاً له لا قصداً لأن الإعتاق وضع لإبطال الملك فثبوت الملك بما وضع لإبطاله يكون تناقضاً والمقتضى تبع للمقتضى فكان حكمه حكم المقتضى والمقتضى وهو الإعتاق لا يوجب فلذا تبعه؛ كذا في المحيط. ولو كان الساكت جماعة فاختار بعضهم السعاية وبعضهم فلكل منهم ما اختار في قول أبي حنيفة ؛ كذا في البدائع واختلف في حد اليسار هنا ففي الهداية، ثم المعتبر يسار التيسير وهو أن يملك من المال قدر نصيب الآخر لا يسار الغني لأن به يقيد له النظر من الجانبين بتحقيق ما قصده المعتق من القربة وإيصال بدل حق الساكت إليه. وجعله في فتح القدير ظاهر الرواية قال : وفي رواية الحسن استثنى الكفاف وهو المنزل والخادم وثياب البدن والذي يظهر أن استثناء الكفاف لا بد منه على ظاهر الرواية ولذا اقتصر عليه في المحيط فقال : ثم حد اليسار أن يكون المعتق مالكاً لمقدار قيمة ما بقي من العبد سوى ملبوسه وقوت يومه لا ما يعتبر في حرمة الصدقة وصححه في المجتبى. وتعتبر قيمة العبد في الضمان والسعاية يوم الإعتاق لأنه سبب الضمان كالغصب، وكذلك يعتبر يسار المعتق وإعساره يوم الإعتاق حتى لو أعتق وهو موسر ثم أعسر لا يبطل حق التضمين، ولو أعتق وهو معسر ثم أيسر لا يثبت لشريكه حق التضمين لأن الضمان متى تعين على المعتق أو السعاية على العبد شرعاً برئ الآخر عن الضمان ولا يعود إليه أبداً كالغاصب مع غاصب الغاصب إذا تعين الضمان على أحدهما باختيار المالك برئ الآخر عنه فكذا هذا. ولو اختلفا في قيمة العبد يوم العتق فإن كان العبد قائماً يقوم العبد للحال لأنه أمكن معرفة قيمته للحال بالعيان ورفع اختلافهما بالبيان، وإن كان العبد هالكاً فالقول قول المعتق لأنه تعذر معرفة قيمته بالعيان لأن أوصافه تتغير بالموت فيجب اعتبار قول واحد منهما والساكت يدعي الزيادة والمعتق ينكر فيكون القول له وإن اتفقا على أن الإعتاق سابق على الاختلاف فالقول قول المعتق كان العبد قائماً أو هالكاً، لأنه وقع العجز عن معرفة قيمته لأن قيمة الشيء قد تزداد وقد تنقص بمضي الوقت فيكون القول قول المعتق لإنكار الزيادة. وإن اختلفا في الوقت والقيمة فقال المعتق أعتقته كذا وقيمته مائة وقال الساكت أعتقته للحال يوم وقيمته مائتان يحكم بالعتق للحال لأن العتق أمر حادث، والأصل في الحوادث أن يحكم بحدوثها حال ظهورها فمن ادعى الحدوث حالة الظهور فهو متمسك بالأصل فيكون القول له فكان العتق ثبت بتصادقهما للحال فيقوم العبد إن كان قائماً ويكون القول للمعتق في قيمته إن كان هالكاً، وكذلك على هذا التفصيل لو اختلف الساكت والعبد في قيمته. وإن اختلفا في يسار المعتق وإعساره والعتق متقدم على الخصومة إن كانت مدة يختلف فيها اليسار والإعسار فالقول قول المعتق لأنه ينكر اليسار وشغل ذمته بالضمان وإن كان لا يختلف يعتبر من وقت للحال، فإن علم يسار المعتق للحال فلا معنى للاختلاف وإن لم يعلم فالقول للمعتق . ولو مات أحدهم قبل أن يختار الشريك شيئاً فلا يخلو إما إن مات العبد أو المعتق أو الساكت، فإن مات العبد ضمن المعتق في ظاهر الرواية لأنه ضمان إتلاف شرع لجبر الفائت فلا يسقط بهلاك محل التلف كما لو هلك المغصوب، وفي رواية لا يضمن المعتق . وإن كان للعبد كسب رجع بما ضمن المعتق فيه لأنه يملك نصيب الساكت بأداء الضمان العتق فصار مكاتباً له. وهل للساكت أن يأخذ من تركة العبد قيمة نصيبه إذا لم يضمن المعتق؟ قيل : له ذلك كالمكاتب وقال عامة مشايخنا : ليس له ذلك وظاهر إطلاق محمد يدل عليه، وأما إذا مات المعتق والعتق في صحته يؤخذ الضمان من ماله وإن كان في مرضه فعندهما لا يجب شيء على ورثته في ماله وعند محمد يستوفي من ماله وأما إذا مات الساكت فلورثته أن يختار والإعتاق أو الضمان أو السعاية لأنهم قائمون مقام مورثهم، فإذا اختار بعضهم العتق وبعضهم الضمان فلهم ذلك في ظاهر الرواية. وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه ليس لهم ذلك وصححه في المبسوط وفي المجتبى ومعنى قوله لورثته الإعتاق الإبراء لا حقيقة العتق لأن المستسعي بمنزلة المكاتب عنده ولا تورث رقبة المكاتب بموت مولاه وإنما يورث
The Book of Emancipation: Chapter on the Slave who is Partially Freed (398-399). In the amount, and the impermissibility of kitabah for more than the value does not indicate an excessive increase, because there is no benefit in it, as the ruling is likewise in the compromise of the silent partner with the co-owner who freed. Al-Bada'i says: If the one who did not free the freed slave makes a compromise for money, this is subject to the divisions we mentioned in kitabah. If the compromise is for dirhams and dinars up to half his value, it is permissible; likewise if it is for less than half his value; likewise if it is for more than half the value which people ordinarily overlook. But if it is for more than his value such that people do not ordinarily overlook, then the excess is void in the opinion of all of them because it is riba. End. So the truth is that the options are five, as in al-Bada'i and others. The author, in his explanation of the partner, gave a general statement that includes immediate and contingent emancipation. Al-Fath al-Qadir says: If he adds it (to a future time), it should not be accepted from him for a long period, because it is like tadbir in meaning. If he made a tadbir, then the slave must work for his freedom immediately, so he is freed as they stated. Thus, it should be added for a period similar to the period of istis'a'. End. The author, by mentioning these options, indicated that he does not have the option of leaving him as he is, because there is no way to benefit from him while freedom is established in a part of him, so he must be brought to freedom as in al-Bada'i. And that if he chooses one of what was mentioned, it is determined. If he chooses istis'a', then he does not have the option of tadmin, and vice versa. Yes, if he chooses istis'a', then he has the option of i'taq. And that the silent partner does not have the option of tadmin in part and sa'yah in part, as in al-Mabsut. And he gave absolute wording in tadmin of the wealthy partner, which is restricted to the case where the emancipation was without his permission. If one of them freed his share with the other's permission, then there is no dhaman upon him; rather, it is istis'a' in the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf: He is liable, because in his view it is a dhaman of ownership, not destruction. Hence, all the wala' belongs to him, and the dhaman of ownership is not dropped by consent. The reasoning for the apparent narration is that the dhaman of emancipation is a dhaman of destruction, and therefore it differs based on wealth and insolvency. Indeed, he only owned his partner's share by virtue of the emancipation to correct it, not intentionally, because emancipation was established to nullify ownership, so establishing ownership by that which was established to nullify it is a contradiction. The incidental follows the essential, so its ruling is the ruling of the essential, and the essential (which is emancipation) does not obligate (ownership), so it follows it. Thus in al-Muhit. If the silent partner is a group, and some choose sa'yah and others [something else], then each has what he chose in the opinion of Abu Hanifa. Thus in al-Bada'i. And there is a difference regarding the definition of wealth (yasar). In al-Hidayah: The considered criterion is yasar al-taysir, which is that he owns from property the amount equal to the other's share, not the wealth of the rich, because by it the view for both sides is restricted to realizing his intention of drawing near (to Allah) and delivering the compensation of the silent partner's right to him. In al-Fath al-Qadir, he considered it the apparent narration. He said: In the narration of al-Hasan, he exempted the necessities (kifaf), which are the house, the servant, and body clothing. And what appears is that the exemption of necessities is inevitable in the apparent narration, and hence al-Muhit limited it to that, saying: The definition of wealth is that the emancipator owns the amount of value of what remains of the slave, aside from his clothing and the food of the day, not what is considered in the prohibition of sadaqah. And al-Mujtaba authenticated it. And the value of the slave is considered for dhaman and sa'yah on the day of emancipation, because it is the cause of dhaman, like usurpation. Likewise, the wealth/insolvency of the emancipator is considered on the day of emancipation, so that if he freed while wealthy and then became insolvent, the right of tadmin is not nullified; and if he freed while insolvent and then became wealthy, the right of tadmin does not become established for his partner, because when the dhaman is determined upon the emancipator or the sa'yah upon the slave by law, the other is discharged from dhaman and it never returns, like the usurper with the usurper's usurper: when the dhaman is determined upon one of them by the owner's choice, the other is discharged from it; so likewise here. And if they differ regarding the value of the slave on the day of emancipation: if the slave is present, he is evaluated at the present time because his value can be known now by sight and their difference resolved by demonstration; and if the slave is dead, the word is that of the emancipator because knowing his value by sight is impossible since his attributes change with death, so the word of one of them must be accepted, and the silent partner claims an increase while the emancipator denies, so the word is his. If they agree that the emancipation preceded the dispute, then the word is that of the emancipator, whether the slave is present or dead, because the inability to know his value occurred because the value of something may increase or decrease with the passage of time, so the word is that of the emancipator for denying the increase. And if they differ regarding the time and the value: the emancipator says 'I freed him at such a time and his value was a hundred', and the silent partner says 'I freed him at the present time and his value was two hundred', the emancipation is judged to have occurred at the present time because emancipation is a new event, and the principle regarding events is that they are judged to have occurred at the time they appear. So whoever claims the occurrence at the time it appeared holds fast to the principle, so his word is accepted. Thus, emancipation is established by their agreement that it is at the present time, so the slave is evaluated if present, and the word of the emancipator regarding his value if dead. And similarly with this detail if the silent partner and the slave differ regarding his value. And if they differ regarding the wealth/insolvency of the emancipator and the emancipation is prior to the dispute: if it is a period in which wealth and insolvency may vary, the word is that of the emancipator because he denies wealth and the burden of dhaman upon his liability; if it does not vary, it is considered from the time to the present. If the emancipator's wealth at present is known, then there is no sense in dispute; if not known, the word is that of the emancipator. And if one of them dies before the partner chooses anything: there is no escape – either the slave dies, or the emancipator, or the silent partner. If the slave dies, the emancipator is liable in the apparent narration because it is a dhaman of destruction legislated to compensate for the lost, so it is not nullified by the destruction of the subject of destruction, as if the usurped property is destroyed. In one narration, the emancipator is not liable. And if the slave has earnings, the [emancipator] recovers what he paid as dhaman, because he owns the silent partner's share by paying the dhaman of emancipation, so he becomes a mukatab for him. And does the silent partner have the right to take the value of his share from the slave's estate if the emancipator did not pay? It was said: He has that like the mukatab, and the majority of our scholars said: He does not have that, and the apparent wording of Muhammad indicates it. As for if the emancipator dies and the emancipation was in health, the dhaman is taken from his estate; if it was in illness, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), nothing is obligatory upon his heirs from his estate; and according to Muhammad, it is taken from his estate. As for if the silent partner dies, his heirs have the option of emancipation or dhaman or sa'yah because they stand in place of their testator. If some choose emancipation and others choose dhaman, they have that in the apparent narration. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that they do not have that, and it was authenticated in al-Mabsut and al-Mujtaba. And the meaning of the statement 'for his heirs [the option of] emancipation' is absolution, not actual emancipation, because the mustas'i is like a mukatab in his view, and the corpus of the mukatab is not inherited upon the death of his master; rather, [the share?] is inherited.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the situation where a jointly-owned slave is partially freed by one co-owner. The author explains the options available to the silent partner (the one who did not free his share). These options are derived from earlier works like al-Bada'i'. The text outlines the ruling on compromise (sulh) regarding the slave's value, emphasizing that excessive amounts beyond what people ordinarily overlook are void due to riba. The true options are five, covering various scenarios. The author also clarifies that the silent partner cannot simply leave the slave as is, because partial freedom necessitates full manumission eventually. The choice is binding: if he chooses istis'a' (the slave working to earn his freedom), he cannot later demand tadmin (compensation from the co-owner). A key point is that tadmin is only applicable when the emancipation was without the silent partner's permission; if permission was given, the slave must work (sa'yah) per the apparent narration. The text then presents a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the nature of the guarantee (dhaman). Abu Yusuf considers it a guarantee of ownership (dhaman tamalluk), so the co-owner who freed acquires full wala' and the liability is not dropped by consent. The apparent ruling, however, treats it as a guarantee of destruction (dhaman itlaf), which varies with the co-owner's wealth. The reasoning is that emancipation is meant to nullify ownership, so acquiring ownership through it is contradictory; the incidental follows the essential. The passage also discusses the definition of wealth (yasar) for the emancipator: it is yasar al-taysir, meaning he must own enough to pay the partner's share, but not the full wealth of a rich person. Some narrations exempt basic necessities. The value of the slave and the emancipator's wealth are considered on the day of emancipation, like in cases of usurpation. If there is a dispute over value, the word of the emancipator is generally preferred, especially if the slave is dead; if the slave is alive, he is evaluated at present. Regarding death of one party: if the slave dies, the emancipator is liable in the apparent narration (like a usurper), though there is a contrary narration. If the emancipator dies, his estate is liable if the emancipation was in health; if in illness, the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ. If the silent partner dies, his heirs inherit the options, but there is a dispute whether they can choose differently among themselves.

Key Terms
السعاية (al-sa'yah): The requirement for the slave to work and earn money to pay the silent partner's share of his value.
التضمين (al-tadmin): The right of the silent partner to demand compensation from the co-owner who freed his share.
اليسار (al-yasar): Wealth condition for the co-owner to be liable for tadmin; defined as owning the amount of the partner's share beyond basic necessities.
الاستسعاء (al-istis'a'): The slave's effort to earn his freedom; synonymous with sa'yah in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), what is the nature of the guarantee (dhaman) when a co-owner frees his share without the other's permission?
A) Guarantee of ownership (tamalluk)
B) Guarantee of destruction (itlaf)
C) Guarantee of wala' (loyalty)
D) No guarantee at all

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the dhaman for partial emancipation is a guarantee of destruction, not ownership, and thus its availability depends on the co-owner's wealth at the time of emancipation.

Guarantee Rights Fixed at Manumission: The Irrevocability of Liability
Ruling with rational proof (qiyas)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding change in wealth of the manumitter and apply the qiyas to similar cases.

Core Idea
The guarantee right is fixed at the moment of manumission and never changes, like the liability of a chosen usurper.

The passage you are about to study establishes a key principle in partial manumission: the financial status of the manumitter is assessed only at the moment of manumission. Later changes in wealth do not affect the rights of the silent partner. The author proves this by analogy to a case involving multiple usurpers, showing that once liability is fixed, it is irrevocable. This lesson will guide you through the ruling, its proof, and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
Read the first part of the passage: 'يسار المعتق وإعساره يوم الإعتاق'. Note that the only relevant time for assessing the manumitter's wealth is the day of manumission.
The rule is clear: later wealth or poverty does not matter.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The passage gives two cases: (1) wealthy at manumission then poor; (2) poor at manumission then wealthy. Determine the ruling for each: in (1) the guarantee right remains; in (2) it does not arise.
The right is either established at the moment or not; later events cannot create or destroy it.
3
Grasp the legal reason (ta'lil)
The author states: 'لأن الضمان متى تعين على المعتق أو السعاية على العبد شرعاً برئ الآخر عن الضمان ولا يعود إليه أبداً'. Once the obligation is fixed on one party (manumitter or slave), the other is freed permanently. This is the underlying rationale.
The obligation is a 'تعين' (fixed assignment) that extinguishes the other's liability.
4
Analyze the qiyas
The analogy is to a usurper and a sub-usurper: when the owner chooses to hold one liable, the other is freed and the liability never returns. Identify the asl (original case: usurpers) and the far' (new case: manumitter/slave), and the illa (common effective cause: fixed liability by choice/law).
The qiyas is explicit: 'كالغاصب مع غاصب الغاصب'.
5
Apply the rule
Now you can apply this rule to any scenario of partial manumission. The manumitter's wealth at the moment of manumission is decisive, and once the path (guarantee or earning) is set, it cannot change. Practice with the examples in the practice tasks.
This principle prevents exploitation and uncertainty in obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسار المعتق وإعساره يوم الإعتاق حتى لو أعتق وهو موسر ثم أعسر لا يبطل حق التضمين، ولو أعتق وهو معسر ثم أيسر لا يثبت لشريكه حق التضمين لأن الضمان متى تعين على المعتق أو السعاية على العبد شرعاً برئ الآخر عن الضمان ولا يعود إليه أبداً كالغاصب مع غاصب الغاصب إذا تعين الضمان على أحدهما باختيار المالك برئ الآخر عنه فكذا هذا.
The wealth and poverty of the manumitter are considered on the day of manumission, such that if he manumitted while wealthy and then became poor, the right of guarantee (against him) is not invalidated; and if he manumitted while poor and then became wealthy, the right of guarantee does not become established for his partner. Because the guarantee, once it is established upon the manumitter or the earning upon the slave by [sacred] law, the other is freed from the guarantee and it never returns to him ever, like the usurper with the usurper of the usurper: when the guarantee is established upon one of them by the owner's choice, the other is freed from it. So this is similar.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a rational proof (qiyas). It establishes a key principle in partial manumission: the financial status (wealth or poverty) of the manumitter is assessed only at the moment of manumission. Later changes do not affect the rights of the silent partner (the one who did not manumit). If the manumitter was wealthy at the time, he must guarantee the silent partner's share; if poor, the slave must earn (sa'y). Once one of these obligations is fixed, it never transfers back to the other party, even if circumstances change. The author proves this by analogy (qiyas) to the case of a usurper and a sub-usurper: when the owner chooses to hold one of them liable, the other is completely freed from liability—the liability does not revert. Thus, here too, the obligation is fixed permanently.

Key Terms
التضمين (al-tadmin): Guarantee/liability: the manumitter's obligation to pay the silent partner the value of his share in the slave.
السعاية (al-sa'ayah): Earning: the slave's obligation to work and earn to pay the silent partner the value of his share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a manumitter is wealthy at the time of manumission but later becomes poor, what happens to the silent partner's right to seek guarantee?
A) The guarantee is invalidated because the manumitter can no longer pay.
B) The guarantee remains valid, and the manumitter must still pay.
C) The slave must now earn (sa'ayah) instead.
D) The silent partner loses his right entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that the obligation in partial manumission is fixed at the moment of manumission and is irrevocable, supported by analogy to the case of a usurper.

Evidentiary Rules in Partial Manumission Disputes
Rules for resolving disputes between partners in partial manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi evidentiary rule for disputes between a manumitter and a silent partner over the slave's value, time of manumission, or the manumitter's wealth, and apply the rule to different factual scenarios.

Core Idea
When objective evidence is unavailable, the manumitter's word is accepted as the denier.

In Hanafi law, when partners in partial manumission disagree about the slave's value, the timing, or the manumitter's wealth, the court must determine whose statement is accepted. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a series of rulings based on the principle that the denier's word is preferred when evidence is uncertain. This lesson unpacks those rulings and their rationales, equipping you to apply them in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of dispute
Determine whether the disagreement concerns the slave's value at the time of manumission, the timing of manumission itself, or the manumitter's financial status (wealth/poverty).
2
Check the availability of objective evidence
Is the slave alive or dead? Has enough time passed to change the value or wealth? If the slave is alive and present, the court can appraise him now. If dead, objective determination is impossible.
3
Apply the ruling based on the evidence situation
When evidence is possible (slave alive, no time lapse), the court uses it. When impossible (slave dead, time passed) or uncertain (dispute over timing), the manumitter's word is accepted because he denies the claim of higher value or wealth.
4
Note the exception: timing dispute
If the manumitter claims an earlier date and the silent partner claims today, the manumission is judged to have occurred today (the present time) because events are attributed to their nearest occurrence. Then value is determined: if slave alive, current value; if dead, manumitter's word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختلفا في قيمة العبد يوم العتق فإن كان العبد قائماً يقوم العبد للحال لأنه أمكن معرفة قيمته للحال بالعيان ورفع اختلافهما بالبيان، وإن كان العبد هالكاً فالقول قول المعتق لأنه تعذر معرفة قيمته بالعيان لأن أوصافه تتغير بالموت فيجب اعتبار قول واحد منهما والساكت يدعي الزيادة والمعتق ينكر فيكون القول له وإن اتفقا على أن الإعتاق سابق على الاختلاف فالقول قول المعتق كان العبد قائماً أو هالكاً، لأنه وقع العجز عن معرفة قيمته لأن قيمة الشيء قد تزداد وقد تنقص بمضي الوقت فيكون القول قول المعتق لإنكار الزيادة. وإن اختلفا في الوقت والقيمة فقال المعتق أعتقته كذا وقيمته مائة وقال الساكت أعتقته للحال يوم وقيمته مائتان يحكم بالعتق للحال لأن العتق أمر حادث، والأصل في الحوادث أن يحكم بحدوثها حال ظهورها فمن ادعى الحدوث حالة الظهور فهو متمسك بالأصل فيكون القول له فكان العتق ثبت بتصادقهما للحال فيقوم العبد إن كان قائماً ويكون القول للمعتق في قيمته إن كان هالكاً، وكذلك على هذا التفصيل لو اختلف الساكت والعبد في قيمته. وإن اختلفا في يسار المعتق وإعساره والعتق متقدم على الخصومة إن كانت مدة يختلف فيها اليسار والإعسار فالقول قول المعتق لأنه ينكر اليسار وشغل ذمته بالضمان وإن كان لا يختلف يعتبر من وقت للحال، فإن علم يسار المعتق للحال فلا معنى للاختلاف وإن لم يعلم فالقول للمعتق.
And if they disagree about the value of the slave on the day of manumission: if the slave is still alive, the slave is valued at the present time because its value can be known now by sight and their disagreement is resolved by demonstration; but if the slave is dead, the word is the word of the manumitter because it is impossible to know his value by sight since his attributes change with death, so it is necessary to accept the word of one of them, and the silent partner claims an increase while the manumitter denies it, so the word is his. And if they agree that the manumission preceded the disagreement, the word is the word of the manumitter whether the slave is alive or dead, because it became impossible to know his value, as the value of a thing may increase or decrease with the passage of time, so the word is the manumitter's due to his denial of the increase. And if they disagree about the time and the value: the manumitter says 'I manumitted him on such a date and his value was one hundred' and the silent partner says 'You manumitted him today and his value was two hundred,' the manumission is judged to have occurred today because manumission is a new event, and the principle regarding events is that they are judged to have occurred at the time of their appearance; so whoever claims the event at the time of its appearance is adhering to the principle, thus the word is his. So the manumission is established by their agreement on the present time, then the slave is valued if he is alive, and the word is the manumitter's regarding his value if he is dead. Similarly, with this detail, if the silent partner and the slave disagree about his value. And if they disagree about the wealth or poverty of the manumitter, and the manumission preceded the litigation, if there is a period in which wealth and poverty can change, the word is the word of the manumitter because he denies being wealthy and thereby having his liability occupied with guarantee; but if it does not change [during that period], it is considered from the present time. If the current wealth of the manumitter is known, there is no meaning to disagreement; if not known, the word is the manumitter's.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents a series of evidentiary rules for resolving disputes between the manumitter (mu'tiq) and the silent partner (sākit) or the slave. The key principle is that the manumitter's word is preferred in situations where the value cannot be objectively determined due to the passage of time or the slave's death. When the slave is alive and present, the court can simply appraise him now. If dead, the manumitter's denial of a higher value is accepted because the silent partner claims an increase. If they agree that the manumission happened earlier but disagree on the value at that time, the manumitter's word is taken because values change over time, and the manumitter denies the claim of higher value. When they disagree on the time of manumission itself, the rule is that the event is presumed to have occurred at the time it is claimed to have appeared (i.e., the present), so the manumitter's claim that it was later may be overridden by the silent partner's claim of a present manumission? Actually the text says: if the manumitter claims he manumitted earlier (kadha) and the silent partner says today, the ruling is that the manumission is judged as today because it is a new event. This seems counterintuitive, but the reasoning: the silent partner asserts the manumission happened now (at the time of litigation), which is the latest possible time, and the principle is that events are attributed to the nearest time. So his claim is upheld for timing. Then the value is determined: if slave alive, current value; if dead, the manumitter's word on value is taken. For disputes about wealth at time of manumission, the manumitter's word is taken if there has been enough time for a change, as he denies being wealthy (thus liable for guarantee). If the time is too short for change, the court looks at current wealth.

Key Terms
القول قول المعتق (al-qawlu qawlu al-mu'tiq): The manumitter's statement is taken as true (with an oath) in disputes over value or wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the slave is dead and the manumitter and silent partner disagree on the slave's value at the time of manumission, whose word is accepted?
A) The silent partner's, because he has a right to compensation.
B) The manumitter's, because he denies the claim of a higher value.
C) The slave's heirs' word.
D) A neutral appraiser's estimate.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when partners in partial manumission disagree over value or wealth, the manumitter's word is accepted whenever objective evidence is unavailable, because he denies the claim of increase.
