Linguistic Implications of Conditional Oaths: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Analyzing the linguistic implications of conditional phrases used in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of two linked conditional oaths using 'إن لم يكن دخل' and 'إن كان دخل' and explain the linguistic reasoning that leads to the difference of opinion within the school.

Core Idea
The phrases 'if he did not enter' and 'if he entered' imply admission of the opposite fact, so both manumission and divorce occur.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of two conditional oaths—one suspended on non-entry and the other on entry. The correct view relies on a keen understanding of Arabic usage in oaths. We will trace the linguistic reasoning that leads to the position that both conditions take effect, and see why some Hanafis disagreed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two conditional oaths
Note the two statements: 'If Zayd did not enter (إن لم يكن دخل) today, my slave is free' and 'If Zayd entered (إن كان دخل) today, my wife is divorced'. The first is suspended on non-entry, the second on entry.
2
Recognize the implied admission in each phrase
According to the relied-upon view, the phrase 'إن لم يكن دخل' is used to affirm that entry actually occurred, rebutting someone who denies it. Similarly, 'إن كان دخل' affirms non-entry. Thus the speaker admits the opposite of the literal condition.
This is different from simpler phrases like 'إن لم يدخل' which have no such implication.
3
Understand the opposing view based on doubt
Some Hanafis (al-Nihayah, al-Tabyin) argue that both conditions are uncertain, so neither consequence occurs. They treat the phrases as purely conditional without implicit admission.
This view highlights the care needed in interpreting oath formulations.
4
Analyze the linguistic rebuttal from the relied-upon view
The author refutes the opposing view by showing the idiomatic usage: 'إن لم يكن دخل' confirms entry in the past (used in debates), and 'إن كان دخل' confirms non-entry. This makes the speaker an admitter of the condition's fulfillment.
This is supported by Fath al-Qadir and Talkhis al-Jami'.
5
Conclude with the practical consequence
The correct view is that both manumission and divorce take effect. The thamarah al-khilaf is that under the opposing view, neither would occur, creating a real-world difference in outcomes.
The tarjih is clear: the relied-upon view is that both occur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أعتقه صاحبي منذ سنة وأعتقته أنا أمس وإن لم يقر بإعتاق نفسه لكن قامت عليه بينة أنه أعتقه أمس فهو ضامن لشريكه لظهور الإعتاق منه بالبينة فدعواه على شريكه العتق المتقدم لا يمنع ظهور الإعتاق منه بالبينة ويمنع ظهوره بإقراره اهـ. وقيد بكون المعلق متعدداً لأنه لو قال عبده حر إن لم يكن فلان دخل هذه الدار اليوم ثم قال امرأته طالق إن كان دخل اليوم عتق وطلقت لأن باليمين الأولى صار مقراً بوجود شرط الطلاق، وباليمين الثانية صار مقراً بوجود شرط العتق . وقيل : لم يعتق ولم تطلق لأن أحدهما معلق بعدم الدخول والآخر بوجوده وكل واحد من الشرطين دائر بين الوجود والعدم فلا ينزل الجزاء بالشك؛ كذا في النهاية، وينبغي أن يفرق بين التعليق بالشرط الكائن وبغير الكائن فيقع في المعلق بالكائن لا بغير الكائن لأن الإقرار يتصور في الكائن دون غيره؛ كذا في التبيين. وهو وما قبله مردودان والحق الأول لأن صيغة إن لم يكن دخل تستعمل لتحقيق الدخول في الماضي رداً على المماري في الدخول وعدمه فكان معترفاً بالدخول وهو شرط الطلاق فوقع بخلاف إن لم يدخل ليس فيها تحقيق وصيغة إن كان دخل ظاهرة لتحقيق عدم الدخول رداً على من تردد فيه فكان معترفا بعدم الدخول وهو شرط وقوع العتق فوقع بخلاف إن دخل فإنه ليس فيها تحقيق أصلاً والحاصل أنه قد اشتبه هذا التركيب على القائل بعدم الوقوع فيهما بتركيب إن لم يدخل وإن دخل ؛ إليه أشار في فتح القدير. وفي تلخيص الجامع باب اليمين التي تنقض صاحبتها حلف بالعتق إن لم يكن دخل أمس وبالطلاق إن كان دخل وقعا لأنه بكل يمين زعم الحنث في الأخرى لهذا لو أعتق أحدهما ثم قال لكل واحد لم أعنك عتقا، ولا يلزم ما لو كانت الأولى والله إذا الغموس لا يدخل تحت الحكم ليكذب به في الأخرى وتمامه فيه.
He said: If my partner freed him a year ago and I freed him yesterday, and he did not confess to freeing himself, but evidence was established against him that he freed him yesterday, then he is liable to his partner because the freeing appears from him by evidence. So his claim against his partner of prior freeing does not prevent the freeing appearing from him by evidence, but it is prevented by his confession. [End]. And it is restricted by the condition that the suspended (mu'allaq) is multiple; because if he said: 'My slave is free if so-and-so did not enter this house today' and then said: 'My wife is divorced if he entered today', then the slave is free and the wife is divorced, because by the first oath he becomes confessing to the existence of the condition for divorce, and by the second oath he becomes confessing to the existence of the condition for manumission. And it was said: He neither frees nor divorces, because one is suspended on non-entry and the other on entry, and each of the two conditions fluctuates between existence and non-existence, so the consequence is not executed due to doubt; thus in al-Nihayah. And it should be distinguished between suspension on an existing condition and on a non-existing one; so it occurs in the one suspended on the existing, not on the non-existing, because confession is conceivable in the existing not in other; thus in al-Tabyin. And both these views are rejected, and the correct is the first, because the phrase 'if he did not enter' is used to confirm entry in the past, in rebuttal of the one disputing entry or its absence, so he is an admitter of entry, which is the condition for divorce, so it occurs, unlike 'if he did not enter' (لم يدخل) which has no confirmation. And the phrase 'if he entered' is apparent for confirming non-entry, in rebuttal of the one who hesitates in it, so he is an admitter of non-entry, which is the condition for manumission, so it occurs, unlike 'if he entered' (إن دخل) which has no confirmation at all. In sum, this construction has been confused by the one who says that neither occurs with the construction 'if he did not enter' and 'if he entered'. This was pointed out in Fath al-Qadir. And in Talkhis al-Jami', chapter on the oath that is nullified by its companion: He swears by manumission 'if he did not enter yesterday' and by divorce 'if he entered', they both occur, because by each oath he claims perjury in the other. For this reason, if he frees one of them, then says to each: 'I did not mean you by the freeing', it does not necessitate that if the first is 'by Allah', the false oath (ghumus) does not fall under the ruling to be falsified by the other. And its completion is there.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of conditional oaths when the conditional phrases are worded in a specific way. The text presents a scenario: two owners of a slave each claim to have freed him at different times. If one owner fails to confess but evidence shows he freed him yesterday, he is liable. The main discussion, however, focuses on a more nuanced case: if a man says, 'If Zayd did not enter the house today, my slave is free' and then immediately says 'If Zayd entered today, my wife is divorced.' According to the relied-upon view (al-haqq al-awwal), both the manumission and divorce take effect. The reasoning is linguistic: the phrase 'if he did not enter' (إن لم يكن دخل) is an idiom used to affirm that he actually did enter, rebutting someone who denies or doubts the entry. Thus it amounts to an admission that entry occurred, fulfilling the condition for divorce. Similarly, 'if he entered' (إن كان دخل) is used to affirm that he did not enter, thus admission of non-entry fulfills the condition for manumission. Other Hanafi authorities like al-Nihayah and al-Tabyin either say neither takes effect (due to doubt) or distinguish based on whether the condition is actually realized. The author refutes these, siding with the position that both conditions take effect. He clarifies that the error arises from confusing these phrasings with simpler ones like 'if he did not enter' (إن لم يدخل) which do not carry such implication. The passage also references the summary of al-Jami' concerning oaths that nullify each other.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission or confession; here it is inferred from the phrasing of the condition, implying the speaker confirms the fact of entry or non-entry.
المعلق (al-mu'allaq): That which is suspended on a condition (ta'liq). Refers to the conditional manumission or divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in this passage, what is the ruling if a man says, 'If Zayd did not enter the house today, my slave is free' and then says, 'If Zayd entered today, my wife is divorced'?
A) The slave is free but the wife is not divorced.
B) The wife is divorced but the slave is not free.
C) Both the slave is free and the wife is divorced.
D) Neither occurs.

Key Takeaway
The specific Arabic conditional phrases used can imply admission of the opposite fact, leading to the fulfillment of the condition in Hanafi law.

Buying a Slave from Contesting Owners Who Claim Manumission
Ruling on validity of purchase and subsequent manumission when two owners claim freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer purchases a slave from two owners who each claim to have freed him, and explain the legal reasoning (qiyas) for the validity of the purchase and the requirement of specification (bayan).

Core Idea
A purchase from contesting owners is valid; one slave is freed, but the buyer must clarify which one.

When two owners both claim to have freed the same slave and then sell him to a third person, it creates a legal puzzle. The Hanafi school rules that the sale is valid even if the buyer knows one owner must be lying. The buyer's belief before ownership is irrelevant; once he owns both shares, one slave (the one actually freed earlier) becomes free, and he must specify which one. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof via analogy, and a sub-case about the buyer's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
You have two owners, each claiming to have freed the same slave. They sell the slave to a third person. The issue: is the purchase valid? Does the slave become free? According to Fath al-Qadir, the purchase is valid even if the buyer knows one claim is false. One slave becomes free upon the buyer, and he is ordered to specify which one.
The buyer does not know which claim is true; the ruling does not depend on that knowledge.
2
Understand the analogy (qiyas)
The author draws an analogy: if a person acknowledges a slave's freedom while the owner denies it, then later buys the slave, the purchase is valid because his claim before ownership is not considered. Here, the buyer's belief before buying is similarly irrelevant. The effective cause (illa) is that the buyer's statement before ownership does not affect the contract.
This qiyas is jali (clear) because the illa is apparent.
3
Distinguish the sub-case when the buyer is unaware
If the buyer does not know about the conflicting oaths, the judge administers oaths to the owners and does not force the buyer to specify unless evidence is established. This shows that the buyer's knowledge affects the procedure but not the validity of the sale.
Al-Muhit provides this detail.
4
Analyze the objection and its resolution
Al-Maqdisi raises a problem: why should the buyer be ordered to specify? The freeing occurred on a specific person (the one actually freed), not an anonymous one, and the ambiguity is not the buyer's fault. The answer from al-Tuhfah: when the freeing occurred, its identity was not known to the parties, so it is treated as freeing an unspecified slave. Hence, the buyer's specification is needed to resolve the ambiguity.
This objection shows the depth of Hanafi legal analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف بعدم عتقهما في مسألة الكتاب إلى أنه لو اشتراهما إنسان صح وإن كان عالماً بحنث أحد المالكين لأن كلاً منهما يبيع عبده، وزعم المشتري في العبد قبل ملكه له غير معتبر كما لو أقر بحرية عبد ومولاه ينكر ثم اشتراه صح، وإذا صح شراؤه لهما واجتمعا في ملكه عتق عليه أحدهما لأن زعمه معتبر الآن ويؤمر بالبيان لأن المقضى عليه معلوم؛ كذا في فتح القدير، وهو يفيد أن أحد المتحالفين لو اشترى العبد من الحالف الآخر فإنه يصح ويعتق عليه أحدهما ويؤمر بالبيان لما ذكر كما لا يخفى. وفي المحيط : هذا إذا علم المشتري بحلفهما، فإن لم يعلم فالقاضي يحلفهما ولا يجبر على البيان ما لم تقم البينة على ذلك اهـ. قوله : (قال لكل واحد لم أعنك عتقاً) لأن قوله للأول لم أعن هذا إقرار منه بوقوع العتق على الثاني، وقوله للآخر بعد ذلك لم أعن هذا إقرار منه بوقوع العتق على الأول فعتقا جميعاً، وهكذا في الطلاق؛ كذا في الخانية. وسيذكر المؤلف المسألة معللة عن الاختيار عند قوله «والبيع والموت» والمتحرير الخ» قوله : (ويؤمر بالبيان لأن المقضى عليه معلوم قال المقدسي في شرحه قلت : وقد أشكل على ذلك فإن العتق نازل في المعين دون المنكر فيجب أن لا يكون البيان للمشتري إذ الإجمال ليس من جهته فينبغي أن يمنع من التصرف فيهما إلى أن يبرهن أحدهما على عتقه كما لو أعتق أحد عبديه ثم نسيه ثم وجدت الإشكال في التحفة وأجاب بأن العتق حال وقوعه لم يدر محله فكان كإعتاق المنكر بخلاف ما إذا أعتق عبداً ثم نسيه لأن العتق نزل في المعلوم.
The author indicated that in the book's issue, both are not freed: that if a person buys them both, it is valid even if he knows that one of the two owners is lying, because each of them sells his slave. And the buyer's claim regarding the slave before ownership is not considered, just as if he acknowledged a slave's freedom while his owner denies it, then he buys him, it is valid. And if his purchase of both is valid and they come into his ownership, one of them becomes free upon him because his claim is now considered, and he is ordered to specify because the one who is adjudged is known; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And it indicates that if one of the two opposing swearers buys the slave from the other swearer, then it is valid and one of them becomes free upon him, and he is ordered to specify as mentioned, as is not hidden. And in al-Muhit: This is if the buyer knows of their swearing; if he does not know, the judge administers oaths to them and does not force him to specify unless evidence is established for that. End. His saying: (He said to each: I did not mean you by the freeing) because his saying to the first 'I did not mean this' is an admission from him that the freeing occurred upon the second, and his saying to the other after that 'I did not mean this' is an admission that the freeing occurred upon the first, so both are freed, and similarly in divorce; thus in al-Khaniyyah. And the author will mention the issue reasoned from al-Ikhtiyar at his saying 'And the sale and death...' and al-Muharrir etc. His saying: (And he is ordered to specify because the one who is adjudged is known). Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: I said: This is problematic because the freeing occurs on a specific person, not on the one who denies, so the specification should not be required from the buyer because the ambiguity is not from his side; rather, he should be prevented from dealing with both until one of them proves his freedom, just as if he freed one of his two slaves and then forgot which one. Then I found the problem in al-Tuhfah, and he answered that when the freeing occurred, its locus was not known, so it is like the freeing of an anonymous person, unlike the case of freeing a specific slave and then forgetting, because the freeing occurred on a known.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the scenario where a third person buys a slave from two owners who each claim to have previously freed the same slave. The ruling, taken from Fath al-Qadir, states that the purchase is valid even if the buyer knows that one of the owners must be lying about the manumission, because each owner is selling his own share. The buyer's belief about the slave's status before ownership does not affect the sale, analogous to acknowledging a slave's freedom while the owner denies it and then purchasing him – the purchase is valid. Once the buyer owns both shares, one of the two slaves (by presumption, the one who was actually freed) becomes free. The buyer is ordered to clarify which one, because he is now in a position where his own admission (that one is free) takes effect. If the buyer is unaware of the oaths, the judge administers oaths to the owners and does not force the buyer to specify without evidence. The commentary then discusses a sub-case: if the buyer says to each slave 'I did not mean you by the freeing', this is taken as admission that the other is free, freeing both. Al-Maqdisi raises an objection: the buyer should be prohibited from dealing with both until proof is established, because the ambiguity is not his fault. The answer from al-Tuhfah is that since the freeing occurred without a known locus, it is like freeing an unspecified slave; hence the buyer is ordered to specify.

Key Terms
البيان (al-bayan): Clarification or specification; the buyer must specify which of the two slaves is the one that was freed.
اليمين الغموس (al-yamin al-ghumus): A deliberate false oath. Mentioned in context that such an oath does not affect the legal consequence of another oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases a slave from two owners who each claim to have freed him, and the buyer knows one is lying, what is the ruling?
A) The purchase is invalid and the buyer must return the slave.
B) The purchase is valid and both slaves become free immediately.
C) The purchase is valid and one slave becomes free; the buyer must specify which.
D) The buyer must wait for a court decision before buying.

Key Takeaway
A purchase from contesting owners is valid; the buyer's knowledge is irrelevant; one slave becomes free, and the buyer must specify which one.

Father's Acquisition of Son's Share: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on father's liability for son's share jointly owned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute regarding a father's liability when acquiring a son's share jointly, and apply the options available to the partner under each view.

Core Idea
Implicit consent frees the father; explicit wealth triggers liability for the Sahibayn.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a father must compensate his partner when he acquires a share of his son jointly. Imam Abu Hanifa holds no liability, reasoning that the father's participation in the purchase implies consent to manumit his share. The Sahibayn require compensation if the father is wealthy. The Thamarah al-Khilaf affects the partner's options (free or istis'aa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its condition
What is the initial ruling stated? Who holds this view? What is the condition of wealth?
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof (qiyas)
How does Abu Hanifa derive no liability? What is the analogy with express permission?
The 'illa is sharing in the cause (purchase).
3
Contrast the Sahibayn's position
When do they impose liability? On what basis?
They tie liability to the father's wealth.
4
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
What practical options does the partner have under each view? When does istis'aa apply?
Abu Hanifa: partner may free or seek istis'aa. Sahibayn: no choice; father pays.
5
Distinguish the case of umm walad
Why is the father always liable for the umm walad? How does this differ?
Guarantee of ownership vs. consent-based.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن ملك ابنه مع آخر عتق حظه ولم يضمن ولشريكه أن يعتق أو يستسعي) لأنه ملك شقص قريبه فعتق عليه ولا ضمان عليه ولو كان موسراً لأنه رضي بإفساد نصيبه كما إذا أذن له بإعتاق نصيبه صريحاً ودلالة ذلك أنه شاركه فيما هو علة العتق وهو الشراء لأن شراء القريب إعتاق وثبت لشريكه الإعتاق أو الاستسعاء لبقائه على ملكه كالمكاتب كما قدمناه. وهذا كله عند الإمام. وقالا في الشراء ونحوه يضمن الأب نصف قيمته إن كان موسراً، الابن لشريك أبيه إن كان معسراً. أطلق المصنف في الملك فشمل ما إذا كان بالشراء أو الهبة أو الصدقة أو الوصية أو الإمهار أو الإرث وشمل ما إذا كان عالماً بأنه ابنه أو لا وهو ظاهر الرواية عنه لأن الحكم يدار على السبب كما إذا قال لغيره كل هذا الطعام وهو مملوك للآمر ولا يعلم الآمر بملكه، وذكر الابن اتفاقي لأن الحكم في كل قريب يعتق عليه كذلك. وقيد بكونه ملكه مع آخر لأنه لو بدأ الأجنبي فاشترى نصفه ثم اشترى الأب نصفه الآخر وهو موسر فالأجنبي بالخيار إن شاء ضمن الأب لأنه ما رضي بإفساد نصيبه، وإن شاء استسعى الابن في نصف قيمته لاحتباس ماليته عنده وهذا عند أبي حنيفة لأن يسار المعتق لا يمنع السعاية عنده وقالا : لا خيار له ويضمن الأب نصف قيمته لأن يسار المعتق يمنع السعاية عندهما . وقيد بالقريب لأنه لو ملك مستولدته بالنكاح مع آخر فإنه يجب عليه ضمان النصف لشريكه كيفما كان وإن كان ملكها بالإرث والفرق أن ضمان أم الولد ضمان تملك وذلك لا يختلف بين أن يكون بصنعه أو بغير صنعه ولهذا لا يختلف باليسار والإعسار . وإنما صح شراء الابن مع آخر في مسألة الكتاب ولم يصح شراء العبد نفسه هو وأجنبي من مولاه بالنسبة إلى حصة الأجنبي لاجتماع العتق والبيع في حق واحد في زمان واحد لأن بيع نفس العبد منه إعتاق على مال فبطل البيع في حصة الأجنبي بخلاف مسألة الكتاب لأن شراء القريب تملك في الزمان الأول وإعتاق في الزمان الثاني. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو حلف أحدهما بعتق عبد إن ملك نصفه فملكه مع آخر فالحكم كذلك وهو على الاختلاف. قوله : (وإن اشترى نصف ابنه ممن يملك ابنه لا يضمن لبائعه لأن البائع شاركه في العلة وهو البيع لأن علة دخول المبيع في ملك المشتري الإيجاب والقبول وقد شاركه فيه. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة موسراً كان أو معسراً. وقالا إن كان الأب موسراً يجب عليه الضمان. قيد بكونه ممن يملك
His saying: (And whoever owns his son jointly with another, his share is freed and he is not liable, and his partner may free or seek istis'aa) because he owned a share of his relative, so it is freed upon him and there is no liability even if he is wealthy, because he consented to the spoiling of his share, just as if he had expressly authorized him to free his share. And the indication of that is that he participated in the cause of freeing, which is purchase, because purchasing a relative is a freeing. And his partner's right to free or seek istis'aa is established because his share remains in his ownership like a mukatab, as we have explained. All this is according to the Imam. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: In purchase and the like, the father guarantees half its value if he is wealthy, but the son's share is for the partner of his father if the father is insolvent. The author made 'ownership' general, including purchase, gift, charity, bequest, dower, inheritance, and including whether he knows it is his son or not, and that is the apparent narration from him because the ruling is based on the cause, just as if he said to another 'Eat this food' while it is owned by the commander and the commander does not know of his ownership. And the mention of the son is incidental because the ruling is the same for any relative who is freed upon him. And it is restricted by his owning it jointly with another, because if a stranger first bought half of him, then the father bought the other half while wealthy, then the stranger has a choice: if he wishes he can hold the father liable because he did not consent to the spoiling of his share, and if he wishes he can seek istis'aa from the son for half his value because his financial capacity is retained with him. This is according to Abu Hanifa because the wealth of the manumitter does not prevent istis'aa according to him. And the two said: He has no choice, and the father guarantees half its value, because the wealth of the manumitter prevents istis'aa according to them. And it is restricted to a relative, because if he owns his umm walad through marriage jointly with another, then he must guarantee half to his partner in any case, even if he owned her through inheritance. The difference is that the guarantee of the umm walad is a guarantee of ownership, and that does not differ whether it is by his action or not, and therefore it does not differ with wealth or insolvency. And the purchase of the son jointly with another is valid in the book's issue, but the purchase of the slave himself jointly with a stranger from his master is not valid regarding the stranger's share, due to the concurrence of freeing and sale in one right at one time, because the sale of the slave himself from him is a freeing for compensation, so the sale is void regarding the stranger's share, unlike the book's issue because the purchase of a relative is ownership in the first time and freeing in the second time. And the author indicated that if one of them swears by freeing a slave if he owns half of him, then he owns him jointly with another, the ruling is the same, and it is according to the difference (of opinion). His saying: (And if he buys half of his son from one who owns his son, he is not liable to the seller, because the seller participated in the cause, which is the sale, because the cause of the sold item entering the buyer's ownership is the offer and acceptance, and the father participated in it. This is according to Abu Hanifa, whether he is wealthy or insolvent. And the two said: If the father is wealthy, liability is necessary. Restricted to being from one who owns...
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a father acquires a share of his son jointly with another person. The text begins with the ruling: if a man owns his son jointly with someone else, the father's share is freed immediately upon acquisition, and he is not liable to the partner for the value, even if wealthy. The partner may either free his own share or demand istis'aa (compensation by the son's labor). This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The reasoning is that the father's act of purchase is itself an act of manumission (since buying a relative effects freedom), and his consent is implicit. The partner's right to istis'aa is similar to the case of a mukatab. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they hold that if the father is wealthy, he must guarantee half the value to the partner. The text then broadens the ruling to include all means of acquisition (purchase, gift, inheritance, etc.) and states that it applies even if the father does not know the person is his son. It also covers the scenario where an outsider first buys half, then the father buys the other half: the outsider may choose between holding the father liable or seeking istis'aa from the son under Abu Hanifa; under the Sahibayn, the father is liable and the outsider has no choice. A distinction is drawn with the case of owning an umm walad (slave mother) jointly: the father is always liable for her value, because the guarantee is one of ownership, not related to wealth. The text also explains why the purchase of a son jointly with another is valid, whereas a slave purchasing himself (with a stranger) is not – because that would combine sale and manumission concurrently. It then mentions that the same difference applies to a conditional oath. Finally, it addresses the specific case of buying half of one's son from someone who owns the entire son: under Abu Hanifa, the father is not liable to the seller, because the seller participated in the 'illa (cause) of the transfer of ownership (offer and acceptance). Under the Sahibayn, if the father is wealthy, liability follows.

Key Terms
الاستسعاء (al-istis'aa): The partner's right to require the freed slave (son) to work to earn the value of the partner's share, since the son's earning capacity remains.
ضمان التملك (daman al-tamalluk): Guarantee of ownership, distinguished from tort liability. Applied to umm walad: father must always guarantee half, regardless of wealth or mode of acquisition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is required when a wealthy father acquires a share of his son jointly with a partner?
A) The father must pay half the value to the partner.
B) The father must free the son entirely.
C) The partner must free his share.
D) The son must work to compensate the partner.

Key Takeaway
When a father acquires a son jointly, the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn centers on whether wealth triggers liability, affecting the partner's options.

Joint Ownership of a Relative: Automatic Freedom and Partner's Options
Ruling on joint ownership of a relative and partner's options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint ownership of a relative, explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the legal consequences.

Core Idea
Owning a relative with a partner frees your share automatically with no compensation — the partner impliedly consented.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key ruling on joint ownership of a relative (such as a son). When a person buys a share of his own relative jointly with another, that share becomes free immediately. The central dispute is whether the father (or relative-owner) must compensate his partner. Imam Abu Hanifa holds no compensation is due, while the Sahibayn require payment if the owner is wealthy and acquired by purchase. The passage also clarifies the partner's subsequent options and the reasoning behind the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
What is the scenario? A person jointly owns a relative (e.g., a son) with another. According to the text, what happens to the relative's share upon ownership? The share becomes free ('ataqa) and the owner does not guarantee (does not owe compensation) to his partner.
The ruling is specific to relatives who would be freed by ownership (e.g., son, father, etc.).
2
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Why is no compensation due? The text gives two reasons: (1) the partner consented to spoiling his share by entering into joint ownership, just as if he explicitly permitted the other to free his share; (2) buying a relative is itself a form of emancipation, so the partner's consent is implied by the act of joining in purchase.
This is based on the Hanafi principle that consent can be implied from conduct (dalala).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
What is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn)? They say: if the father acquired the son by purchase and is wealthy, he must pay half the value to his partner. If insolvent, no payment. This differs from Abu Hanifa's unconditional no-compensation rule.
The dispute centers on whether wealth affects the partner's implied consent.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
According to the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) and the commentator, the relied-upon position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa: no compensation even if wealthy. The partner's options are to free his own share or demand istis'a' (the slave works to earn the partner's share value).
The Sahibayn's view is considered but not adopted as the operative fatwa in this context.
5
Apply the distinctions and exceptions
The passage clarifies that the ruling applies only to relatives (e.g., son, father) and not to an umm walad (slave mother of a child), where compensation is always due. It also contrasts the validity of buying a son jointly vs. a slave buying himself jointly with another.
Understanding these boundaries prevents incorrect analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولشريكه أن يعتق أو يستسعي وإن اشترى نصف ابنه ممن يملك كله لا يضمن لبائعه ويسعى قوله : (ومن ملك ابنه مع آخر عتق حظه ولم يضمن ولشريكه أن يعتق أو يستسعى) لأنه ملك شقص قريبه فعتق عليه ولا ضمان عليه ولو كان موسراً لأنه رضي بإفساد نصيبه كما إذا أذن له بإعتاق نصيبه صريحاً ودلالة ذلك أنه شاركه فيما هو علة العتق وهو الشراء لأن شراء القريب إعتاق وثبت لشريكه الإعتاق أو الاستسعاء لبقائه على ملكه كالمكاتب كما قدمناه. وهذا كله عند الإمام. وقالا في الشراء ونحوه يضمن الأب نصف قيمته إن كان موسراً، الابن لشريك أبيه إن كان معسراً. أطلق المصنف في الملك فشمل ما إذا كان بالشراء أو الهبة أو الصدقة أو الوصية أو الإمهار أو الإرث وشمل ما إذا كان عالماً بأنه ابنه أو لا وهو ظاهر الرواية عنه لأن الحكم يدار على السبب كما إذا قال لغيره كل هذا الطعام وهو مملوك للآمر ولا يعلم الآمر بملكه، وذكر الابن اتفاقي لأن الحكم في كل قريب يعتق عليه كذلك. وقيد بكونه ملكه مع آخر لأنه لو بدأ الأجنبي فاشترى نصفه ثم اشترى الأب نصفه الآخر وهو موسر فالأجنبي بالخيار إن شاء ضمن الأب لأنه ما رضي بإفساد نصيبه، وإن شاء استسعى الابن في نصف قيمته لاحتباس ماليته عنده وهذا عند أبي حنيفة لأن يسار المعتق لا يمنع السعاية عنده وقالا : لا خيار له ويضمن الأب نصف قيمته لأن يسار المعتق يمنع السعاية عندهما . وقيد بالقريب لأنه لو ملك مستولدته بالنكاح مع آخر فإنه يجب عليه ضمان النصف لشريكه كيفما كان وإن كان ملكها بالإرث والفرق أن ضمان أم الولد ضمان تملك وذلك لا يختلف بين أن يكون بصنعه أو بغير صنعه ولهذا لا يختلف باليسار والإعسار . وإنما صح شراء الابن مع آخر في مسألة الكتاب ولم يصح شراء العبد نفسه هو وأجنبي من مولاه بالنسبة إلى حصة الأجنبي لاجتماع العتق والبيع في حق واحد في زمان واحد لأن بيع نفس العبد منه إعتاق على مال فبطل البيع في حصة الأجنبي بخلاف مسألة الكتاب لأن شراء القريب تملك في الزمان الأول وإعتاق في الزمان الثاني. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو حلف أحدهما بعتق عبد إن ملك نصفه فملكه مع آخر فالحكم كذلك وهو على الاختلاف.
And for his partner he may free or seek earning, and if he buys half of his son from one who owns him entirely, he does not guarantee to his seller, and he seeks earning. He said: (Whoever owns his son with another, his share becomes free and he does not guarantee, and for his partner he may free or seek earning) because he owned a share of his relative, so it becomes free upon him, and there is no guarantee upon him even if he is wealthy, because he consented to spoiling his share, just as if he explicitly permitted him to free his share; and the indication of that is that he shared with him in what is the cause of freedom, which is purchase, because buying a relative is freedom, and his partner is established to have freedom or seeking earning because he remains under his ownership like a mukatab as we have mentioned. And all this according to Imam. And they two said in purchase and its like: the father guarantees half its value if he is wealthy, and if he is insolvent, not. The author generalized in ownership so it includes cases of purchase, gift, charity, bequest, dower, or inheritance; and it includes whether he knew him to be his son or not, and it is the apparent transmission from him because the ruling revolves on the cause, just as if he said to another, 'Eat all this food' while it is owned by the one commanding and he does not know of his ownership. And mentioning the son is by agreement because the ruling is similarly for every relative who becomes free upon him. And he conditioned it being owned by him with another because if the outsider initiated by buying half then the father bought the other half while wealthy, the outsider has a choice: if he wishes he may hold the father responsible because he did not consent to spoiling his share, and if he wishes he may seek earning from the son in half of his value due to retention of his monetary value with him, and this according to Abu Hanifa because the wealth of the manumitter does not prevent the earning according to him. And they two said: he has no choice, and the father guarantees half of his value because the wealth of the manumitter prevents the earning according to them. And he conditioned it by being a relative because if he owned his umm walad by marriage with another, then he must guarantee the half to his partner in any case, even if he owned her by inheritance; and the difference is that the guarantee of the umm walad is a guarantee of ownership, and that does not differ between being by his action or without his action, and therefore it does not differ by wealth or insolvency. And the purchase of the son with another is valid in the issue of the book, while the purchase of the slave himself and a stranger from his master is not valid regarding the stranger's share due to the combination of freedom and sale in one right at one time, because the sale of the slave himself to him is an emancipation for money, so the sale becomes void regarding the stranger's share, unlike the issue of the book because buying a relative is ownership in the first time and emancipation in the second time. And the author indicated that if one of them swore by freeing a slave if he owns half of it, and then he owned it with another, the ruling is the same, and it is upon the difference.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq. It addresses the scenario where a person owns a son jointly with another person. The ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa is that the son's share becomes free automatically without any compensation due from the father to his partner, even if the father is wealthy, because the partner consented to potential loss by entering into joint ownership. The partner then has two options: he may free his own share (resulting in the son's full freedom), or he may demand the son work to earn the value of the partner's share (istis'a'). The Sahibayn differ: if the father acquired the son through purchase and is wealthy, he must pay half the value to his partner. The commentary further generalizes the rule to all modes of acquisition and clarifies that it applies only to relatives who would be freed by ownership (like sons). It contrasts this with a case where the father owns an umm walad jointly, where compensation is always due. Additionally, it explains why buying a son jointly with another is valid while a slave buying himself jointly with a stranger is not. The passage confirms that the same ruling applies when the joint ownership results from a vow or oath.

Key Terms
استسعاء (istis'ā'): The partner's right to have the slave work to earn the value of his share when part of the slave becomes free automatically.
شقص (shiqs): A share or portion of a jointly owned slave.
رضي بإفساد نصيبه (raḍī bi-ifṣād naṣībihi): Consented to the spoiling (loss) of his share, a key legal basis for no compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a man jointly owns his son with a partner and the son becomes free, what is the partner's right?
A) The partner must automatically free his share without compensation.
B) The partner may either free his share or demand istis'ā' from the son.
C) The partner must demand compensation from the father.
D) The partner has no rights; the son is fully free.

Key Takeaway
Joint ownership of a relative triggers automatic freedom of the owner's share without compensation, based on implied consent, and the partner retains the right to free or seek istis'ā'.

Buying Half of One's Son: No Guarantee to Seller (Abu Hanifa's View)
Ruling on buying half of one's son from a sole owner and liability to seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father buying half of his son from a third party and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding liability to the seller.

Core Idea
If a father buys his son, the seller's participation in the sale removes liability for compensation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario: a father buys half of his son from a person who owns the son entirely. The central question is whether the father must compensate the seller for the emancipation that results. Imam Abu Hanifa's position is that no compensation is due, regardless of the father's wealth, because the seller himself was a participant in the cause of emancipation. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold a different view: if the father is wealthy, he must pay. Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a father buying half of his son from a person who owns the son entirely. The ruling according to the matn is that the father does not guarantee (compensate) the seller.
The ruling is conditional on the seller being the sole owner (mman yamliku).
2
Understand the 'illa (legal cause)
The passage states: the seller shared with the father in the cause (al-illa) of the purchase, which is the offer and acceptance. This cause leads to the son entering the father's ownership, and ownership of a relative triggers emancipation. By participating in the cause, the seller is deemed to have consented to the emancipation, so no compensation is due.
The cause (illa) is central – the seller's own action (the sale) brings about the emancipation.
3
Note the disagreement of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree: if the father is wealthy (mūsir), they say he must pay the guarantee (al‑ḍamān) to the seller. The condition of wealth is irrelevant in Abu Hanifa's view.
The Sahibayn argue that the seller did not intend to give away his property for free when the father is capable of paying.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Assume Zaid owns a slave named Amr. Bakr, Amr's father, buys half of Amr from Zaid. According to Abu Hanifa, Bakr owes nothing to Zaid, even if Bakr is rich. According to the Sahibayn, if Bakr is rich, he must pay Zaid the value of the half.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) arises when the father is wealthy.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: when the father is wealthy, Abu Hanifa says no payment; the Sahibayn require payment. This affects whether the seller receives compensation or not.
This is an intra‑Hanafi ikhtilaf; the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن اشترى نصف ابنه ممن يملك ابنه لا يضمن لبائعه لأن البائع شاركه في العلة وهو البيع لأن علة دخول المبيع في ملك المشتري الإيجاب والقبول وقد شاركه فيه. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة موسراً كان أو معسراً. وقالا إن كان الأب موسراً يجب عليه الضمان. قيد بكونه ممن يملك
He said: (And if he buys half of his son from one who owns his son, he does not guarantee to his seller because the seller shared with him in the cause, which is sale, because the cause of the sold item entering the buyer's ownership is the offer and acceptance, and he has shared in it. And this is according to Abu Hanifa, whether he is wealthy or poor. And they two said: if the father is wealthy, he must pay the guarantee. He conditioned it being from one who owns.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage explains the second part of the matn: when a father buys half of his son from a person who owns the son entirely, the father does not owe any guarantee to the seller, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, regardless of whether the father is wealthy. The reasoning is that the seller himself participated in the cause of emancipation, because the act of sale (offer and acceptance) causes the son to enter the father's ownership, and ownership of a relative triggers emancipation. Therefore, the seller is considered to have consented to the emancipation, so no compensation is due. The Sahibayn disagree: if the father is wealthy, he must pay the seller the value of the share. The commentary ends with a note that the ruling is conditioned on the seller owning the son entirely (not a co-owner).

Key Terms
علة (ʿilla): The legal cause (ratio legis) that leads to the ruling; here the cause of emancipation is ownership through purchase.
ضمان (ḍamān): Financial liability or compensation; in this context, the payment owed by the father to the seller.
بيع (bayʿ): Sale; the contract that transfers ownership in exchange for consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when a father buys half of his son from a third party who owns the son entirely, what is the ruling on compensation to the seller?
A) The father must pay half the value if wealthy
B) The father must pay regardless of wealth
C) The father owes nothing because the seller shared in the cause of emancipation
D) The father must seek istis'ā' from the son

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa holds that the seller's participation in the cause of the sale negates any liability for compensation, even if the father is wealthy.

Judge's Order for Clarification in Ambiguous Manumission
Ruling on judge ordering clarification when a slave is freed but identity is unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a slave is freed without specification and explain the distinction between manumission with unknown locus and forgotten specification.

Core Idea
When a slave is freed without specification, the judge may restrict dealings until clarification, treating it as manumission of an unknown.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq engages with a procedural problem: if a person frees one of his slaves without specifying which, and later the slaves come into the possession of another party, should the judge order clarification from that party? Al-Maqdisi raises an objection based on the nature of manumission, and al-Tuhfah provides a key distinction resolving the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The judge orders clarification (bayān) because the person against whom the ruling is given (the buyer) is known and the ambiguity needs resolution.
The ruling is from the matn 'al-Tahrir' quoted.
2
Consider the objection
Al-Maqdisi argues that manumission applies to a specific slave (the one intended by the owner), so the buyer should not be required to clarify; instead, he should be prevented from dealing with both until proof.
The analogy is to a case where a freed slave is forgotten.
3
Analyze the resolution
Al-Tuhfah answers that when the manumission occurs without known locus (the slave not specified), it is like manumission of an unknown, so the judge can restrict dealings. This differs from forgetting after specifying because in that case the manumission already attached to a specific individual.
The key is the time of manumission: unknown at that moment vs. known but later forgotten.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحرير الخ» قوله : (ويؤمر بالبيان لأن المقضى عليه معلوم قال المقدسي في شرحه قلت : وقد أشكل على ذلك فإن العتق نازل في المعين دون المنكر فيجب أن لا يكون البيان للمشتري إذ الإجمال ليس من جهته فينبغي أن يمنع من التصرف فيهما إلى أن يبرهن أحدهما على عتقه كما لو أعتق أحد عبديه ثم نسيه ثم وجدت الإشكال في التحفة وأجاب بأن العتق حال وقوعه لم يدر محله فكان كإعتاق المنكر بخلاف ما إذا أعتق عبداً ثم نسيه لأن العتق نزل في المعلوم.
And al-Tahrir etc. He said: (And he is ordered to clarify because the one against whom the ruling is given is known.) Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: I say: And this is problematic because the manumission applies to the specific, not the unknown, so the clarification should not be required from the buyer since the ambiguity is not from his side; rather, he should be prevented from dealing with both until one of them proves his manumission, just as if he freed one of his slaves and then forgot [which one]. Then the problem is found in al-Tuhfah, and he answered that the manumission at the time it occurred did not have a known place, so it is like manumission of an unknown, unlike if he freed a slave and then forgot [which one], because the manumission applied to a known [slave].
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage addresses a procedural issue: when a judge orders a party to clarify which slave was freed in a case of ambiguous manumission. The commentator raises a problem: if a man frees one of his slaves without specifying, the manumission is considered to have fallen on a particular slave (the one intended), so why should the buyer (or party) be required to clarify? The analogy is given of a person who frees one of two slaves and then forgets which one. The solution from al-Tuhfah is that when the manumission occurred without known location, it is treated like the manumission of an unknown slave (so the judge can require clarification or restrict dealing). However, if the manumission was of a known slave and then forgotten, the ruling is different because the manumission already attached to a specific individual.

Key Terms
البيان (al-bayān): The act of clarifying or specifying which slave was freed in a case of ambiguous manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when a man frees one of his two slaves without specifying which, and the slaves are in the possession of a buyer?
A) The judge declares both slaves free.
B) The judge orders the buyer to clarify which slave he believes was freed.
C) The judge prevents the buyer from dealing with both until proof.
D) The judge frees the slave chosen by the buyer.

Key Takeaway
When a slave is freed without specification, the judge orders clarification from the party in possession, treating the manumission as having an unknown locus, unlike cases where a specific slave is later forgotten.

Tadbir and Manumission by Co-owners: Three-Owner Case
Ruling on tadbir and manumission by co-owners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rights of a silent partner and a mudabbir when a slave is jointly owned by three, with one giving tadbir and another freeing.

Core Idea
Tadbir is divisible, giving each co-owner distinct rights to compensation.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a scenario where a slave is owned by three wealthy owners. One gives tadbir (posthumous manumission), another frees the slave outright. The third owner, who did nothing, is called the 'silent partner'. What rights does each have? The Hanafi ruling, based on the divisibility of tadbir, grants specific claims for compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their actions
Three owners: one gives tadbir, one manumits, one is silent (does neither).
2
Determine the silent partner's right against the mudabbir
The silent partner can hold the mudabbir liable for the value of his share as a regular slave (one-third), because tadbir impairs his share.
The guarantee is a guarantee of compensation (ḍamān muʿāwaḍa).
3
Determine the mudabbir's right against the manumitter
The mudabbir can hold the manumitter liable for a third of the slave as a mudabbar (i.e., the value with the tadbir condition).
The mudabbir cannot claim the part already guaranteed by the silent partner.
4
Understand why the manumitter is not directly liable to the silent partner
The silent partner may not hold the manumitter directly because the manumitter's act is akin to making the slave a mukatab, requiring consent.
The silent partner's recourse is only against the mudabbir.
5
Note the basis: divisibility of tadbir
Tadbir is divisible according to Imam Abu Hanifa, so each share can be independently affected.
This is a Hanafi-specific view; other schools may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتق باب العبد يعتق بعضه عبد الموسرين دبره واحد وحرره آخر ضمن الساكت المدبر والمدبر المعتق ثلثه مدبر إلا ابنه قوله : (عبد الموسرين دبره واحد وحرره آخر ضمن الساكت المدبر والمدبر المعتق ثلثه مدبر إلا ما ضمن) أي لو كان عبد بين ثلاثة دبره أحدهم ثم أعتقه آخر فللساكت وهو الذي لم يدبر ولم يحرر أن يضمن المدبر وليس له أن يضمن المعتق وللمدبر أن يضمن المعتق ثلث العبد مدبراً وليس له أن يضمنه الثلث الذي ضمنه للساكت وإنما يضمن الساكت المدبر ثلث قيمته قناً لأن التدبير يتجزئ عند الإمام لأنه شعبة من شعبه فيكون معتبراً به فاقتصر على نصيبه، وقد أفسد بالتدبير نصيب الآخرين فكان لكل واحد منهما أن يدبر نصيبه أو يعتق أو يكاتب أو يضمن المدبر أو يستسعي العبد أو يتركه على حاله، فلما حرره الآخر تعين حقه فيه وسقط اختياره غيره فتوجه للشريك الساكت سبباً ضمان تدبير المدبر وإعتاق المعتق فله تضمين المدبر ليكون الضمان ضمان معاوضة إذ هو الأصل حتى جعل الغصب ضمان معاوضة على أصلنا وأمكن ذلك في التدبير لكونه قابلاً للنقل من ملك إلى ملك وقت التدبير وليس له تضمين المعتق لأن العبد عند ذلك مكاتب أو على اختلاف الأصلين، ولا حر بد من رضا المكاتب بفسخه حتى يقبل الانتقال. ثم إن الشريك الذي أعتق نصيبه أفسد على المدبر نصيبه مديراً والضمان يتقدر بقدر المتلف ولا يضمنه قيمة ما ملكه بالضمان من جهة الساكت لأن ملكه ثبت مستنداً وهو ثابت من وجه دون وجه فلا يظهر في حق التضمين، وقد استفيد من كلام المصنف أنه لو كان بين اثنين دبره أحدهما ثم حرره الآخر فللمدبر تضمين المعتق ثلثه مدبراً إن كان موسراً، ولو كان حرره أحدهما ثم دبره الآخر فللمدبر أن يستسعي العبد في نصف قيمته مدبراً لأنه بالتدبير اختار ترك الضمان، ولو لم يعلم أيهما أولاً فإن للمدبر تضمين المعتق ربع القيمة واستسعى العبد في ربع القيمة ويرجع المعتق بما ضمن قوله: (فللمدبر تضمين المعتق ثلثه مدبراً كذا في النسخ ومثله في النهر، والصواب إبدال الثلث بالنصف كما هو ظاهر وقد نبه على ذلك أيضاً أبو السعود محشي مسكين فقال: الصواب أن
Book of Manumission, Chapter on the Slave who is Partly Free. A slave of wealthy owners: one gave tadbir and another freed him. The silent partner holds the mudabbir liable, and the mudabbir holds the manumitter liable for a third of him as mudabbar, except his son. He said: (A slave of wealthy owners: one gave tadbir and another freed him; the silent partner holds the mudabbir liable, and the mudabbir holds the manumitter liable for a third of him as mudabbar, except what he guaranteed.) That is, if there is a slave among three, one of them gave tadbir, then another freed him. So for the silent one, who did not give tadbir nor free, he may hold the mudabbir liable, and he may not hold the manumitter liable. And for the mudabbir, he may hold the manumitter liable for a third of the slave as mudabbar, and he may not hold him liable for the third that he guaranteed to the silent one. The silent partner only holds the mudabbir liable for a third of his value as a regular slave because tadbir is divisible according to Imam, for it is a branch of branches, so it is considered accordingly, and he limited it to his share. And he (the mudabbir) corrupted by tadbir the shares of the others, so each of them had the right to give tadbir for his share, or free, or make a mukatab, or hold the mudabbir liable, or seek istis'ā' from the slave, or leave him as is. When the other freed him, his right became fixed and his other choices fell away, so for the silent partner there arises a cause for guarantee of the mudabbir's tadbir and the manumitter's freeing; thus he may hold the mudabbir liable so that the guarantee is a guarantee of compensation, for that is the principle, as we consider even usurpation as a guarantee of compensation in our school, and that is possible in tadbir because it is transferable from ownership to ownership at the time of tadbir. And he may not hold the manumitter liable because the slave then is like a mukatab or upon a difference of principles. And there is no escape from the mukatab's consent to its rescission until he accepts the transfer. Then, the partner who freed his share corrupted the mudabbir's share as mudabbar, and the guarantee is measured by the amount of damage, and he does not guarantee the value that he owned by guarantee from the side of the silent one because his ownership is established as contingent, and it is established from one aspect not another, so it does not appear in the context of guarantee. And it is derived from the author's speech that if it was between two, one gave tadbir then the other freed him, then for the mudabbir to hold the manumitter liable for a third of him as mudabbar if he is wealthy; and if one freed him then the other gave tadbir, then for the mudabbir to seek istis'ā' from the slave for half of his value as mudabbar because by tadbir he chose to leave the guarantee; and if it is not known which occurred first, then for the mudabbir to hold the manumitter liable for a quarter of the value and the slave seeks istis'ā' for a quarter of the value, and the manumitter takes recourse for what he guaranteed. He said: (For the mudabbir to hold the manumitter liable for a third of him as mudabbar) so it is in copies, and similarly in al-Nahr, and the correct is to replace the third with the half, as is apparent, and Abu al-Sa'ud, the commentator of Miskeen, also noted that and said: The correct is that...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage discusses a complex scenario: a slave is owned by three wealthy owners. One owner gives tadbir (posthumous manumission), another owner frees the slave outright. The silent partner (the third owner who did neither) has the right to hold the mudabbir liable for the value of his share (one-third of the slave's value as a regular slave, because tadbir is divisible). The mudabbir in turn can hold the manumitter liable for one-third of the slave as a mudabbar (i.e., the value of the slave with the condition of tadbir). The reasoning is that tadbir is a legal act that affects all shares, and each partner can choose various options. When one frees, the options of the mudabbir become limited. The commentary also discusses variations: if only two owners, the mudabbir can hold the manumitter liable for half (correcting the text from third to half). If the manumission precedes tadbir, the mudabbir may seek istis'ā' from the slave. If the order is unknown, a proportional solution is given.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbīr): A contract by which a master declares that his slave will be free upon the master's death.
ضمان معاوضة (ḍamān muʿāwaḍa): A guarantee of compensation for damage or loss of property, as opposed to a guarantee of a debt.
استسعاء (istis'ā'): Requiring the slave to earn his freedom price through work when partially free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the three-owner case, who can the silent partner hold liable?
A) The manumitter only
B) The mudabbir only
C) Both the mudabbir and the manumitter
D) Neither, because tadbir is invalid

Key Takeaway
Tadbir's divisibility gives the silent partner a claim only against the mudabbir, not the manumitter.

7 min
Divisibility of Tadbir: The Hanafi Rationale
Rationale for divisibility of tadbir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof that tadbir is divisible and how it forms the basis for liability rules.

Core Idea
Tadbir is divisible because it is a branch of manumission; branches can be split among shares.

The previous lesson established the rulings; now we delve into the underlying legal reasoning (ta'lil). Why does the Hanafi school hold that tadbir is divisible? The passage in Bahr al-Raiq states that 'tadbir is divisible according to Imam, for it is a branch of branches.' This lesson unpacks that proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim about tadbir
The text says 'التدبير يتجزئ عند الإمام' – tadbir is divisible according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
This is the key point of departure from other schools.
2
Understand the reasoning: tadbir is a branch of manumission
The proof: 'لأنه شعبة من شعبه' – it is a branch of the branches of manumission. Branches (shu'ab) can be divided, as they are subordinate parts.
The analogy is to other divisible legal acts like sale or gift.
3
Apply the divisibility to co-ownership
Because tadbir is divisible, each co-owner's share is independently affected. The mudabbir's tadbir only applies to his share but impairs the others' shares, giving them rights.
This justifies why the silent partner and the mudabbir have claims.
4
Contrast with an indivisible view
If tadbir were indivisible, the mudabbir's act would either apply to the whole slave or none. That would leave the silent partner with a claim against the manumitter instead.
The Hanafi view is more nuanced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتق باب العبد يعتق بعضه عبد الموسرين دبره واحد وحرره آخر ضمن الساكت المدبر والمدبر المعتق ثلثه مدبر إلا ابنه قوله : (عبد الموسرين دبره واحد وحرره آخر ضمن الساكت المدبر والمدبر المعتق ثلثه مدبر إلا ما ضمن) أي لو كان عبد بين ثلاثة دبره أحدهم ثم أعتقه آخر فللساكت وهو الذي لم يدبر ولم يحرر أن يضمن المدبر وليس له أن يضمن المعتق وللمدبر أن يضمن المعتق ثلث العبد مدبراً وليس له أن يضمنه الثلث الذي ضمنه للساكت وإنما يضمن الساكت المدبر ثلث قيمته قناً لأن التدبير يتجزئ عند الإمام لأنه شعبة من شعبه فيكون معتبراً به فاقتصر على نصيبه، وقد أفسد بالتدبير نصيب الآخرين فكان لكل واحد منهما أن يدبر نصيبه أو يعتق أو يكاتب أو يضمن المدبر أو يستسعي العبد أو يتركه على حاله، فلما حرره الآخر تعين حقه فيه وسقط اختياره غيره فتوجه للشريك الساكت سبباً ضمان تدبير المدبر وإعتاق المعتق فله تضمين المدبر ليكون الضمان ضمان معاوضة إذ هو الأصل حتى جعل الغصب ضمان معاوضة على أصلنا وأمكن ذلك في التدبير لكونه قابلاً للنقل من ملك إلى ملك وقت التدبير وليس له تضمين المعتق لأن العبد عند ذلك مكاتب أو على اختلاف الأصلين، ولا حر بد من رضا المكاتب بفسخه حتى يقبل الانتقال. ثم إن الشريك الذي أعتق نصيبه أفسد على المدبر نصيبه مديراً والضمان يتقدر بقدر المتلف ولا يضمنه قيمة ما ملكه بالضمان من جهة الساكت لأن ملكه ثبت مستنداً وهو ثابت من وجه دون وجه فلا يظهر في حق التضمين، وقد استفيد من كلام المصنف أنه لو كان بين اثنين دبره أحدهما ثم حرره الآخر فللمدبر تضمين المعتق ثلثه مدبراً إن كان موسراً، ولو كان حرره أحدهما ثم دبره الآخر فللمدبر أن يستسعي العبد في نصف قيمته مدبراً لأنه بالتدبير اختار ترك الضمان، ولو لم يعلم أيهما أولاً فإن للمدبر تضمين المعتق ربع القيمة واستسعى العبد في ربع القيمة ويرجع المعتق بما ضمن قوله: (فللمدبر تضمين المعتق ثلثه مدبراً كذا في النسخ ومثله في النهر، والصواب إبدال الثلث بالنصف كما هو ظاهر وقد نبه على ذلك أيضاً أبو السعود محشي مسكين فقال: الصواب أن
Book of Manumission, Chapter on the Slave who is Partly Free. A slave of wealthy owners: one gave tadbir and another freed him. The silent partner holds the mudabbir liable, and the mudabbir holds the manumitter liable for a third of him as mudabbar, except his son. He said: (A slave of wealthy owners: one gave tadbir and another freed him; the silent partner holds the mudabbir liable, and the mudabbir holds the manumitter liable for a third of him as mudabbar, except what he guaranteed.) That is, if there is a slave among three, one of them gave tadbir, then another freed him. So for the silent one, who did not give tadbir nor free, he may hold the mudabbir liable, and he may not hold the manumitter liable. And for the mudabbir, he may hold the manumitter liable for a third of the slave as mudabbar, and he may not hold him liable for the third that he guaranteed to the silent one. The silent partner only holds the mudabbir liable for a third of his value as a regular slave because tadbir is divisible according to Imam, for it is a branch of branches, so it is considered accordingly, and he limited it to his share. And he (the mudabbir) corrupted by tadbir the shares of the others, so each of them had the right to give tadbir for his share, or free, or make a mukatab, or hold the mudabbir liable, or seek istis'ā' from the slave, or leave him as is. When the other freed him, his right became fixed and his other choices fell away, so for the silent partner there arises a cause for guarantee of the mudabbir's tadbir and the manumitter's freeing; thus he may hold the mudabbir liable so that the guarantee is a guarantee of compensation, for that is the principle, as we consider even usurpation as a guarantee of compensation in our school, and that is possible in tadbir because it is transferable from ownership to ownership at the time of tadbir. And he may not hold the manumitter liable because the slave then is like a mukatab or upon a difference of principles. And there is no escape from the mukatab's consent to its rescission until he accepts the transfer. Then, the partner who freed his share corrupted the mudabbir's share as mudabbar, and the guarantee is measured by the amount of damage, and he does not guarantee the value that he owned by guarantee from the side of the silent one because his ownership is established as contingent, and it is established from one aspect not another, so it does not appear in the context of guarantee. And it is derived from the author's speech that if it was between two, one gave tadbir then the other freed him, then for the mudabbir to hold the manumitter liable for a third of him as mudabbar if he is wealthy; and if one freed him then the other gave tadbir, then for the mudabbir to seek istis'ā' from the slave for half of his value as mudabbar because by tadbir he chose to leave the guarantee; and if it is not known which occurred first, then for the mudabbir to hold the manumitter liable for a quarter of the value and the slave seeks istis'ā' for a quarter of the value, and the manumitter takes recourse for what he guaranteed. He said: (For the mudabbir to hold the manumitter liable for a third of him as mudabbar) so it is in copies, and similarly in al-Nahr, and the correct is to replace the third with the half, as is apparent, and Abu al-Sa'ud, the commentator of Miskeen, also noted that and said: The correct is that...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage discusses a complex scenario: a slave is owned by three wealthy owners. One owner gives tadbir (posthumous manumission), another owner frees the slave outright. The silent partner (the third owner who did neither) has the right to hold the mudabbir liable for the value of his share (one-third of the slave's value as a regular slave, because tadbir is divisible). The mudabbir in turn can hold the manumitter liable for one-third of the slave as a mudabbar (i.e., the value of the slave with the condition of tadbir). The reasoning is that tadbir is a legal act that affects all shares, and each partner can choose various options. When one frees, the options of the mudabbir become limited. The commentary also discusses variations: if only two owners, the mudabbir can hold the manumitter liable for half (correcting the text from third to half). If the manumission precedes tadbir, the mudabbir may seek istis'ā' from the slave. If the order is unknown, a proportional solution is given.

Key Terms
شعبة (shu'bah): A branch or subcategory of a legal concept; here, tadbir is a branch of manumission.
تجزئ (tajazzu'): Divisibility; the quality of being able to be split into parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof that tadbir is divisible?
A) The Quran explicitly states it.
B) Tadbir is a branch of manumission, which can be divided.
C) The silent partner's right proves it by default.
D) It is based on consensus of all schools.

Key Takeaway
Tadbir's divisibility stems from its nature as a branch of manumission, enabling proportional liability among co-owners.

Two-Owner Cases and Correction of the Text
Correction and order-dependency in two-owner tadbir/manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling to two-owner scenarios and correct the common textual error from 'third' to 'half'.

Core Idea
In two-owner cases, the mudabbir's claim on the manumitter is half, not a third.

The source passage includes a correction: in the two-owner scenario, the mudabbir should hold the manumitter liable for half of the slave as mudabbar, not a third. The commentator and Abu al-Sa'ud note this emendation. This lesson also covers scenarios where the order of acts (tadbir then manumission, or reverse, or unknown) affects the rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two-owner scenario
When only two owners: one gives tadbir, the other manumits. The text originally said 'third' but the correct is 'half'.
The correction applies because each owns half.
2
Understand the correction
The commentator says 'الصواب إبدال الثلث بالنصف' – the correct is to replace third with half.
This is a known textual correction in later commentaries.
3
Examine the order-dependent cases
If tadbir then manumission: mudabbir holds manumitter for half as mudabbar. If manumission then tadbir: mudabbir can only seek istis'ā' from the slave for half. If unknown: proportional solution (quarter each).
The order determines whether the mudabbir's option to claim compensation is available.

Partial Emancipation by Co-Owners: Divisibility and Recourse
Commentary on multiple scenarios of partial manumission by three co-owners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of multiple acts of partial manumission (itq, tadbir, kitaba) by co-owners and apply the rules of recourse and wala' in scenarios of known and unknown order.

Core Idea
Acts of manumission are divisible (muta jazzi'a) according to Abu Hanifa, so each co-owner's act is valid and leads to proportionate wala' and liability.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles the complex scenario where three co-owners each perform a different type of manumission (itq, tadbir, kitaba) on a single slave. The central question is whether these acts are valid and how liability and wala' are distributed. The Hanafi position, following Abu Hanifa, affirms divisibility (tajazzu'), leading to specific rules of recourse that depend on the order of acts and the nature of the guarantee (daman jinaya vs daman tamlik).

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of manumission acts
The three acts are itq (complete emancipation), tadbir (freedom promised upon the owner's death), and kitaba (contract of manumission). Each has distinct legal effects and is performed by a co-owner on his share of the slave.
These acts are considered partial because the slave is co-owned.
2
Understand the Hanafi principle of tajazzu' (divisibility)
According to Abu Hanifa, acts of manumission are divisible (muta jazzi'a). This means each co-owner's act is valid and effective only on his share, and multiple acts can coexist on the same slave without cancellation.
This principle is contested by other schools; the Hanafi school firmly adopts it.
3
Distinguish between known and unknown order
When the chronological order of acts is known, specific recourse rules apply. If the order is unknown, all acts are treated as simultaneous, and the slave is considered free from all shares at once.
The passage uses the conjunction 'waw' to indicate sequence ('then') in the matn.
4
Apply the rules of recourse and wala'
The mudabbar who guarantees the silent partner's share can recoup from the slave as a qinn (ordinary slave), not as a mudabbar, because the guarantee is daman jinaya, not tamlik. Wala' is divided proportionally to the shares of freedom.
The value for recourse is calculated based on the slave's value at the time of the act.
5
Analyze the objection and rebuttal about the value of recourse
Some students argued that when the mudabbar acquires the silent partner's share by guarantee, it should become mudabbar, allowing recourse for two-thirds as mudabbar. The rebuttal: the acquired share remains qinn because the emergence of ownership now does not create tadbir; tadbir is divisible and requires a prior declaration.
The rebuttal reinforces that the guarantee is for injury, not ownership transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ضمن ولو قال لشريكه هي أم ولدك وأنكر تخدمه يوماً وتتوقف يوماً وما لأم ولد هو فلا اعتبار بحاله من اليسار والإعسار ولم يذكر المصنف رجوع المدبر بما ضمنه للساكت على العبد وقد نص الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي بأنه يرجع على العبد بثلث قيمته قناً كما ضمن . وقيد المصنف بكون الساكت اختار تضمين المدبر بعد تحرير الآخر لأنه لو اختار تضمين المدبر . قبل أن يعتقه الآخر ثم أعتقه كان للمدبر أن يضمن المعتق ثلثي قيمته لأن الإعتاق وجد بعد تملك المدبر نصيب الساكت فله أن يضمنه ثلث قيمته قناً ، ثلث قيمته مدبراً كما صفته . قال في فتح القدير : وأورد بعض الطلبة على هذا أنه ينبغي أن يضمنه قيمة ثلثيه مدبراً لأنه حين ملك ثلث الساكت بالضمان صار مديراً لا قناً، ولذا قلنا في وجه كون ثلثي الولاء له لأنه صار كأنه دبر ثلثيه ابتداء، والجواب لا يتم إلا بمنع كون الثلث الذي ملكه بالضمان للساكت صار مدبراً بل هو قن على ملكه إذ لا موجب لصيرورته مدبراً لأن ظهور الملك الآن لا يوجبه والتدبير يتجزئ وذكرهم إياه في وجه كون ثلثي الولاء له غير محتاج إليه إذ يكفي فيه أنه على ملكه حين أعتقه الآخر وأدى الضمان وإنما لم يكن ولاؤه له لما ذكرنا من أنه ضمان جناية لا تملك ا هـ. وبما قررناه أولاً علم أن الواو في قول المصنف «وحرره آخر» بمعنى ثم قيد به لأنه لو أعتقه أحدهم ودبره الآخر وكاتب الآخر ولا يعلم الأول فالتصرفات كلها جائزة ويسعى العبد للمدبر في سدس قيمته وضمن له المعتق أيضاً سدس قيمته مدبراً إن كان موسراً، ويسعى العبد في المكاتبة للثالث، فإن عجز فهو بالخيار، إن شاء استسعى العبد في ثلث قيمته والولاء أثلاثاً، وإن شاء ضمن المدبر المعتق ثلث قيمته نصفين إذا كانا موسرين والولاء بينهما نصفان لأنهما لما جهلا التاريخ يجعل كأن هذه التصرفات وقعن معاً، وأنها متجزئه عند أبي حنيفة فصحت . ثم لا شيء للمعتق على أحد، وإن أعتق واحد وكاتب له على أحد، وإن عجز يرجع على المدبر بثلث قيمته لا على المعتق وتمام تفريعاته في المحيط .
What is guaranteed... and if he said to his partner she is your umm walad and he denied, she serves one day and stops one day. And nothing for umm walad... so no consideration of his state of wealth or poverty. And the author did not mention the return of the mudabbar for what he guaranteed to the silent partner against the slave. Al-Hakim al-Shahid stated in al-Kafi that he returns against the slave for one-third of his value as a slave (qinn) as he guaranteed. And the author restricted it to the silent partner choosing to make the mudabbar liable after the other's emancipation, because if he chose to make the mudabbar liable before the other emancipates him, then the other emancipates, then the mudabbar may guarantee the manumitter two-thirds of the slave's value, because the emancipation occurred after the mudabbar owned the silent partner's share, so he may guarantee him one-third of the value as a slave, one-third as a mudabbar according to its description. In Fath al-Qadir: Some students raised an objection that he should guarantee him the value of two-thirds as a mudabbar because when he owned the silent partner's third by guarantee, he became a mudabbar, not a slave. Hence we said regarding the two-thirds of wala' being for him because he is as if he made tadbir for two-thirds initially. The answer is only complete by denying that the third which he owned by guarantee for the silent partner became mudabbar; rather it is a slave (qinn) in his ownership, since there is no cause for it becoming mudabbar because the emergence of ownership now does not necessitate it, and tadbir is divisible. And their mention of it in the regard of two-thirds of wala' being for him is unnecessary, for it is sufficient that it is in his ownership when the other emancipated and paid the guarantee; his wala' was not for him because it is a guarantee of injury, not ownership. End. And by what we established first, it is known that the waw in the author's statement 'and another freed him' means 'then', restricted by it because if one of them freed him, and the other made him mudabbar, and another made him mukatab, and the first is not known, then all acts are valid. The slave works for the mudabbar for one-sixth of his value, and the manumitter also guarantees him one-sixth of his value as a mudabbar if he is wealthy. And the slave works for the third regarding the kitaba. If he fails, he has the choice: if he wishes, he makes the slave work for one-third of his value and the wala' is in thirds; if he wishes, he makes the mudabbar guarantee the manumitter one-third of the value in half if both are wealthy, and the wala' is half between them because since they are ignorant of the date, it is as if these acts occurred together, and they are divisible according to Abu Hanifa, so they are valid. Then nothing is for the manumitter against anyone. If one freed, and another made kitaba, and the third made tadbir simultaneously, none has recourse because each act occurred in his own property. If one made tadbir first, then the second freed, then the other made kitaba, then the mudabbar has recourse against the manumitter for the value of his share, and the mukatab has no recourse against anyone. If he made tadbir, then kitaba, then freed, the ruling for the mudabbar and manumitter is as we mentioned. As for the mukatab, if the slave fails, he returns against the manumitter for the value of his share because he became a slave for him and the manumitter destroyed him. If he made kitaba first, then tadbir, then freed, and the slave does not fail, he becomes free and no liability on anyone. If he fails, he returns against the mudabbar for one-third of his value, not against the manumitter. And all its branches are in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the chapter on a slave who is partially emancipated. It begins by discussing a case where a co-owner (the 'silent partner' or 'sakit') chooses to hold the mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the owner's death) liable for the value of the slave after another co-owner has freed his share. The passage explains that the mudabbar can recoup from the slave one-third of his value as a qinn (ordinary slave), because the mudabbar's guarantee is akin to a guarantee of injury (daman jinaya) and does not effect ownership. There is an objection from some students that the mudabbar should be able to recover two-thirds as a mudabbar, but the author refutes this by noting that the third acquired through guarantee remains qinn, not mudabbar, because the emergence of ownership now does not necessitate tadbir, and tadbir is divisible. The passage then clarifies that the conjunctive 'waw' in the matn phrase 'wa harrarahu akhar' means 'then' (thumma), indicating sequence. It proceeds to detail various scenarios with three co-owners: one performs itq (emancipation), one tadbir, one kitaba (contract of manumission). If the order is unknown, all acts are valid, with specific rules for compensation and wala' (clientage). The principle that these acts are divisible (muta jazzi'a) according to Abu Hanifa is key. The passage ends by noting that further branches are found in al-Muhit.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by the owner that a slave will be free upon the owner's death; the slave is called mudabbar.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; here it refers to the mudabbar's guarantee of the value of the slave's share to the silent partner.
ولاء (wala'): Clientage, the relationship of inheritance and loyalty between the manumitter and the freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If three co-owners simultaneously perform itq, tadbir, and kitaba on a slave, what is the ruling regarding recourse?
A) The mudabbar can claim against the manumitter.
B) The manumitter can claim against the mudabbar.
C) No one has recourse because each acted within his own property.
D) The mukatab can claim against both.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, acts of partial manumission by co-owners are valid and divisible, with recourse and wala' determined by the order of acts and the nature of the guarantee.
